Refutation of the Seventh Argument: The Verse 'Amintum Man Fi al-Sama'
Imam Razi refutes the seventh argument from the verse 'Are you secure from He who is in the heaven?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation of Q. 67:17 by showing it cannot be taken literally about Allah.

Core Idea
The verse 'He who is in the heaven' does not prove Allah's spatial location.

The opponents of divine transcendence often cite the verse 'Are you secure from He who is in the heaven?' (Q. 67:17) as evidence that Allah is physically located above. Imam Razi dismantles this argument by offering alternative interpretations and showing internal consistency with other verses. This lesson unpacks his two-part refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the arguments stand out.
The passage is dense with two distinct lines of reasoning.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Razi presents two arguments: first from another verse (Q. 43:84), second from the possibility that the verse refers to angels or dominion.
Each argument is introduced with 'First:' and 'We grant... but...'
3
Understand the first argument
Razi cites Q. 43:84 which mentions Allah in heaven and earth. Since 'in the earth' is not literal settlement, 'in the heaven' must be figurative as well.
This is an argument based on consistency of scriptural language.
4
Understand the second argument
Even if taken literally, the phrase 'man fi al-sama' could refer to angels, not Allah, since nothing specifies it is Allah. Alternatively, it could refer to Allah's dominion.
These are plausible interpretations that preserve transcendence.
5
Reflect on the significance
This refutation shows that scripture does not support spatial location for Allah, and that apparent verses must be interpreted in light of clear principles.
This is a key lesson in tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي تمسكوا به سابعا . وهو قوله تعالى : « أم أمنتم من في السماء » (٩٠) فجوابه : إنه لا يمكن إجراء هذه الآية على ظاهرها . ويدل عليه و جهان : الأول : إنه قال : « وهو الذى في السماء إله ، وفى الأرض إله » (٩١) وهذا يقتضى أن يكون المراد من كونه في السماء ، ومن كونه في الأرض معنى واحدا . ولما كان كونه في الأرض ليس بمعنى الاستقرار، فكذلك كونه في السماء ، يجب أن لا يكون بمعنى الاستقرار سلمنا . أنه يمكن إجراء هذه الآية على ظاهرها . لكنا نقول بموجبه : فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من « أم أمنتم من في السماء » (٩٢) ؟ الملائكة الذين هم في السماء ؟ لأنه ليس في الآية (٩٣) ما يدل على أن الذى فى السماء هو الإله لا الملائكة . ولا شك أن الملائكة أعداء الكفار والفساق . سلنا . أن المراد هو الله تعالى ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من « أم أمنتم من في السماء » : ملكة ؟ وخص السماء بالذكر لأنها أعظم من الأرض تفخيم للشأن .
And as for what they held onto, seventh: His statement, the Most High: 'Or are you secure from He who is in the heaven?' (90). Its answer: It is not possible to take this verse according to its apparent meaning. This is indicated by two aspects: First: He said: 'And He is the One who in the heaven is God, and on the earth is God' (91). This requires that the meaning of His being in the heaven and His being on the earth be one meaning. And since His being on the earth is not in the sense of settling, so likewise His being in the heaven must not be in the sense of settling. We grant that it is possible to take this verse according to its apparent meaning. But we say by its implication: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of 'Or are you secure from He who is in the heaven?' (92) be the angels who are in the heaven? Because there is nothing in the verse (93) indicating that the one in the heaven is God and not the angels. And there is no doubt that the angels are enemies of the disbelievers and sinners. We grant that the intended meaning is Allah the Most High, but why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of 'Or are you secure from He who is in the heaven?' be a kingship? And the heaven is specified because it is greater than the earth, for magnifying the matter.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage refutes the seventh argument from the verse 'Am amintum man fi al-sama'' (Q. 67:17). The opponents claim it indicates Allah is spatially located in heaven. Imam Razi gives two arguments. First, by analogy with another verse (Q. 43:84) where Allah is described as 'in the heaven' and 'on the earth', the meaning must be consistent: 'in the earth' does not mean physical settlement, so 'in the heaven' does not either. Second, even if taken literally, the verse could refer to angels, not Allah, as there is no explicit indication it is Allah, and angels are enemies of disbelievers. Alternatively, it could refer to Allah's dominion or kingship (malk) over heaven, with a metaphorical interpretation.

Key Terms
سماء
استقرار
ملائكة
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'man fi al-sama'' literally as Allah being physically in heaven.
Correction: Imam Razi shows the verse can refer to angels or dominion, and another verse confirms figurative language.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why can't the verse 'Am amintum man fi al-sama'' be taken literally about Allah?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because another verse uses similar language about both heaven and earth, and 'in the earth' doesn't mean settlement.
C) Because the word 'man' can only refer to angels.
D) Because the verse is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Am amintum man fi al-sama'' does not prove Allah's spatial location; it is interpreted figuratively or as referring to angels or dominion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'Are you secure from He who is in the heaven?', do you instinctively imagine a physical being above you? How can you train your mind to immediately recall that Allah is not confined to space, and what does that mean for how you relate to Him?
?
Imam Razi used another verse (43:84) to argue for consistent interpretation. Think of an ambiguous verse you have encountered. Did you pause to find a parallel or did you accept a literal meaning? What step can you take this week to be more thorough in understanding Allah's speech?
?
The warning in the verse is very real even if it refers to angels or Allah's dominion. Do you feel the same urgency when you read such verses now that you know they can be interpreted figuratively? What needs to change in your heart to maintain awe of Allah without imagining Him in a place?

Refuting the Hijab Argument: Metaphor Not Physical Veil
Imam Razi refutes the eighth argument using the word 'hijab' as implying physical veil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the anthropomorphic argument based on the word 'hijab' (veil) by explaining its metaphorical meaning as the prevention of vision.

Core Idea
The word 'hijab' does not imply a physical barrier but metaphorically means the prevention of seeing.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a common argument that the Quranic term 'hijab' implies a physical veil behind which Allah is hidden, thus suggesting a location. He refutes this by demonstrating that 'hijab' can be understood metaphorically as the act of preventing vision itself, without any physical barrier. Let's see how he uses the rhetorical concept of majaz to defend God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of refutation.
2
Identify the opponents' claim
The opponents hold onto the word 'hijab' as implying a physical veil. What is their underlying assumption?
3
Understand Imam Razi's refutation
He suggests that 'hijab' can mean 'the absence of vision' itself. Why does he say this is permissible?
He uses the relationship between cause and effect.
4
Reflect on the metaphor
The metaphor works by calling the cause (the veil) by the name of the effect (prevention). How does this make the argument of location irrelevant?
If 'hijab' means prevention, then no physical barrier or direction is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي تمسكوا به ثامنا . و هو لفظ الحجاب . فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من الحجاب : عدم الرؤية. وذلك لأن الحجاب يقتضى المنع من الرؤية ، فكان إطلاق لفظ الحجاب على المنع من الرؤية : مجازا من باب إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب .
And as for what they held onto, eighth: the word 'hijab' (veil). Its answer: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of 'hijab' be the absence of vision? That is because the veil necessitates prevention from vision, so the use of the word 'hijab' for the prevention from vision is a metaphor of the type of calling the cause by the name of the effect.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage refutes the eighth argument based on the word 'hijab' (veil) appearing in the Quran (e.g., Q. 7:46). The opponents claim that a veil implies a physical barrier, thus God must have a location behind the veil. Imam Razi answers that 'hijab' can metaphorically mean 'the prevention of seeing' itself, not a physical object. This is a common rhetorical figure (majaz) where the name of the cause (the veil) is used for the effect (prevention). Thus, no physical barrier or location is implied.

Key Terms
حجاب
منع
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'hijab' in the Quran always refers to a physical curtain or barrier.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that 'hijab' can be a metaphor for the prevention of vision itself, not a physical object.

Checkpoint
What is the essence of Imam Razi's response to the 'hijab' argument?
A) The hijab proves Allah has a direction.
B) The hijab is a metaphor for the prevention of seeing, not a physical object.
C) The hijab is only for angelic beings.
D) The hijab is a clear proof of anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation of 'hijab' by showing it metaphorically means the prevention of seeing, thus no physical barrier or location is implied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across Quranic verses that seem to describe Allah with physical attributes, do you immediately assume a literal meaning, or do you pause to consider the possibility of metaphor as Razi does here? What does your default interpretation reveal about your understanding of Allah's transcendence?
?
Razi uses a cause-effect metaphor to shift from a physical veil to the prevention of seeing. In your own life, are there concepts about Allah that you have taken too literally, limiting your understanding of His greatness? How can you apply Razi's method of metaphorical interpretation to other attributes like 'yad' or 'wajh'?
?
This passage shows that a single word can be reinterpreted to protect divine transcendence. Are you willing to let go of a comfortable, literal image of Allah in order to uphold tanzih? What would that cost you in terms of your current beliefs or practices?

Refuting the Ninth Argument: Raising is Metaphorical
Imam Razi refutes the ninth argument about 'raising' verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Quranic verses about 'raising' to Allah are metaphorical expressions of honor, not indications of direction or spatial location.

Core Idea
Raising to Allah means honor, not movement.

Opponents of divine transcendence often cite verses that mention 'raising' things to Allah, like Jesus being raised (Q. 4:158) or good deeds ascending (Q. 35:10). They argue this implies a physical direction. Imam Razi here offers a clear refutation: these verses are metaphorical, as shown by linguistic usage and parallel Quranic expressions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' argument
Recognize why the verses about raising (raf') are used as evidence for direction. The opponents claim that 'raising to Allah' implies a spatial direction—upwards.
Verses like 'bal rafa'ahu Allahu ilayhi' are their primary proof.
2
See the refutation: metaphorical interpretation
Imam Razi explains that 'raising' here means elevation in honor and rank, not physical movement. He uses the analogy of a king promoting a subject to a high station—we say 'raised to the king' even though no spatial movement occurs.
The key term is 'مجاز' (metaphor).
3
Connect to the supporting verse about 'muqarrabun'
The concept of 'bringing near' (like in Q. 56:10-11) also implies honor and closeness, not spatial proximity. This reinforces the metaphorical reading.
Both 'رفع' and 'تقريب' are used metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذى تمسكوا به تاسعا . وهو الآيات المشتملة على الرفع كقوله تعالى : « بل رفعه الله إليه » (٩٤) ، وقوله : « والعمل الصالح يرفعه » (٩٥) . فالجواب : إن الله تعالى لما رفعه إلى موضع الكرامة ، ومكان آخر، صبح . على سبيل المجاز - أن يقال : إن الله تعالى رفعه إليه . كما أن الملك - إذا عظم منصب إنسان (٩٦) حسن أن يقال : إنه رفعه من تلك الدرجة إلى درجة عالية، وأنه قربه (٩٧) من نفسه. ومنه قوله تعالى: « والسابقون السابقون : أولئك المقربون » (٩٨) .
And as for what they held onto, ninth: the verses that include 'raising' (raf'), such as His statement: 'Nay, Allah raised him up to Himself' (94) and His statement: 'And righteous deed raises it' (95). The answer: Since Allah Almighty raised him to a place of honor and another place, it became permissible - by way of metaphor - to say that Allah Almighty raised him to Himself. Just as a king, when he magnifies the rank of a person (96), it is good to say: He raised him from that degree to a high degree, and that he brought him near (97) to himself. And from that is His statement: 'And those foremost in faith, they are the ones brought near' (98).
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage refutes the ninth argument from verses about 'raising' (raf'), such as Q. 4:158 (Jesus raised to Him) and Q. 35:10 (good deeds raised). Opponents argue that 'raising to Allah' implies direction. Imam Razi responds that this is metaphorical: raising to Allah means raising to a station of honor and closeness, not physical movement. He gives an analogy: when a king promotes someone, we say they are raised to the king, meaning dignity, not spatial displacement. He also cites Q. 56:10-11 about those brought near (muqarrabun), which implies honor, not location.

Key Terms
رفع
مقربون
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that verses about 'raising' to Allah prove that Allah is literally above in a spatial direction.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that 'raising' is a metaphor for honor and exaltation, not physical movement, as demonstrated by the analogy of a king and the verse about 'those brought near.'

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi explain the meaning of 'raising' in verses like 'bal rafa'ahu Allahu ilayhi'?
A) It means literal physical raising to heaven.
B) It is metaphorical for honoring and bringing near.
C) It refers only to Jesus, not to other things.
D) It proves Allah is spatially above.

Key Takeaway
Quranic expressions of 'raising' to Allah are metaphors for honor and closeness, not literal direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah as physically 'above' or 'near' in a spatial way, despite knowing He is beyond direction? What specific verse or thought triggers that feeling, and what will you do next time it arises to remind yourself that it is a metaphor for honor?
?
The king analogy shows that honor can be expressed as 'raising to oneself'. In your daily life, how do you measure your closeness to Allah? Is it based on actions that elevate your rank, or do you still think in terms of physical distance? What concrete step will you take this week to increase your spiritual rank?
?
Are you willing to let go of a literal understanding of any Quranic verse that conflicts with Allah's transcendence, even if it feels comforting? If so, which verse is hardest for you, and when will you intentionally replace that comfort with tawheed?

Refuting Spatial Nearness: 'Indiyya' as Honor
Razi refutes the tenth argument by reinterpreting 'indiyya' as honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the argument from verses using 'ind' (with) by interpreting it as honor rather than spatial nearness.

Core Idea
When Allah says 'with', He means honor, not location.

Imam Razi now tackles the tenth argument based on verses that say something is 'with Allah' (indallah). Opponents claim this implies spatial presence. Razi offers a simple yet powerful answer: 'indiyya' here denotes honor and elevation, not physical nearness. He supports this with two hadith qudsi that clearly show Allah's 'withness' is about care and attention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let Razi's own argument come through before diving into analysis.
Notice that the passage cuts off at the end; we only have the beginning of the refutation.
2
Identify the two hadith
Find the two hadith qudsi that Razi cites. Note how each uses 'ind' (with) in a clearly non-spatial sense.
3
Understand why this refutation is stronger
Razi claims his interpretation is stronger because even the opponents' own verses (about angels) can be understood as honor. Reflect on how this undermines the literalist reading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي تمسكوا به عاشرا . وهو الآيات المشتملة على لفظ العندية : فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد بالعندية : العندية بالشرف والدليل عليه : قوله عليه السلام - حكاية عن رب العزة : « أنا عند المنكسرة قلوبهم لأجلى » ، وقوله : « أنا عند ظن عبدي بي » بل هذا أقوى لان النصوص التي ذكروها تدل على أن الملائكة عند الله وهذه النصوص
And as for what they held onto, tenth: the verses that include the word 'indiyya' (withness). So why is it not permissible that the intended meaning by 'indiyya' is the 'indiyya' of honor? The evidence for it: his statement, peace be upon him - narrating from the Lord of Might: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken for My sake', and his statement: 'I am with the assumption of My servant about Me'. Rather, this is stronger, because the texts they mentioned indicate that the angels are 'with Allah' and these texts...
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage refutes the tenth argument from verses using the word 'ind' (with) to describe something being 'with Allah', such as 'When (ind) your Lord' etc. Opponents argue this implies spatial presence. Imam Razi answers that 'indiyya' can mean honor and closeness in rank, not physical location. He supports this with two hadith qudsi: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken for My sake' and 'I am with the assumption of My servant about Me', where 'with' clearly means care and attention, not spatial presence. He notes that even the texts the opponents mention about angels being 'with Allah' actually support this metaphorical interpretation, but the text is cut off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When the Quran says something is 'with Allah', it means Allah is physically present or contains that thing.
Correction: Razi shows that 'indiyya' can mean honor and special status, as proven by hadith where Allah says 'I am with' those whose hearts are broken, which is clearly not physical.

Checkpoint

A) Physical proximity in heaven.
B) Honor and exaltation, not location.
C) Being inside the throne.
D) It cannot be interpreted and must be taken literally.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the spatial interpretation of 'indiyya' by showing it means honor, supported by prophetic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Response to the Eighth Argument: Hijab
Imam Razi refutes the eighth argument by reinterpreting 'hijab' metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi refutes the claim that the Quranic word 'hijab' implies Allah has a direction or spatial location, by reinterpreting it as a metaphor.

Core Idea
"Hijab" in the verse means prevention of sight, not a physical curtain.

In this lesson, we examine Imam al-Razi's response to the eighth argument used by proponents of direction (jiha) for Allah. They cite the Quranic mention of a 'hijab' (curtain) as evidence that Allah is behind a barrier, implying spatial location. Imam al-Razi offers a linguistic and rational rebuttal: the word is used metaphorically to mean the absence of sight itself, not a physical partition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic source passage once through. Then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of Imam Razi's response.
The passage is an objection-and-answer format.
2
Identify opponent's claim
The opponent argues that the Quranic word 'hijab' (curtain) proves Allah is spatially located behind a barrier. Find this claim in the passage.
3
Understand the refutation
Imam Razi responds by asking why 'hijab' cannot mean the prevention of sight. Understand that the prevention (effect) is caused by the curtain (cause), and the word is metaphorically applied to the effect.
This is a metaphor of cause-for-effect.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this linguistic analysis removes the need for a physical barrier or direction. The verse does not prove spatial location for Allah.
This protects divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذي تمسكوا به ثامنا . و هو لفظ الحجاب . فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من الحجاب : عدم الرؤية. وذلك لأن الحجاب يقتضى المنع من الرؤية ، فكان إطلاق لفظ الحجاب على المنع من الرؤية : مجازا من باب إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب .
And as for what they held onto eighthly, which is the word al-hijab, the answer to it is: why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of al-hijab is the absence of sight? This is because al-hijab necessitates the prevention of sight, so the application of the word al-hijab to the prevention of sight is a metaphor (majaz) of the type of applying the name of the cause to the effect.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that the Quranic mention of a 'hijab' (curtain) proves that God is spatially located behind a barrier. The answer rejects this by reinterpreting 'hijab' as a metaphor. The author explains that the real meaning of 'hijab' is prevention from seeing. Since the curtain (the cause) prevents sight, calling the prevention itself 'hijab' is a common metaphorical usage where the cause names the effect. Thus, the verse does not imply a physical curtain or spatial direction.

Key Terms
حجاب
مجاز
سبب
مسبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'hijab' in the Quran always refers to a physical curtain or barrier.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that 'hijab' is used metaphorically to mean the prevention of sight itself, not a physical object.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's response to the eighth argument, what is the intended meaning of 'hijab' in the relevant verse?
A) A physical curtain preventing vision
B) The prevention of sight itself
C) A veil of light
D) A spatial separation

Key Takeaway
The Quranic word 'hijab' does not imply a physical barrier for Allah; it is a metaphor for the prevention of sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's explanation of metaphor challenge your assumptions about what is 'safer' for faith? Will you commit to re-examining other verses you might have interpreted literally without considering metaphorical meanings?
?

?

Refuting the Ninth Argument: 'Raf'' is Metaphorical for Honor
Imam Razi refutes the ninth Quranic proof for direction by interpreting 'raf'' metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Quranic verses mentioning 'raising' to God do not imply physical direction but are metaphors for honor and rank.

Core Idea
Raising to God in the Quran means elevation in status, not space.

This lesson examines the ninth proof used by those who claim God has a physical direction. They cite verses where things are 'raised' to God, such as Jesus and good deeds. Imam Razi refutes this by showing that 'raf'' is a common Arabic metaphorical expression for granting honor, just as a king raises a subject's rank. The lesson will walk through his reasoning and the supporting Quranic example.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's proof
Identify which verses the opponent uses and why they think it implies direction.
The verses are from Surah Nisa 4:158 and Surah Fatir 35:10.
2
Understand Razi's answer
Razi says the language is metaphorical. Focus on the phrase 'on the basis of metaphor'.
3
Analyze the king analogy
Notice how the analogy clarifies: when a king raises someone's rank, we say 'brought him near' without physical movement.
Keep in mind that 'bringing near' is also metaphorical.
4
Consider the Quranic support
Razi quotes 'al-muqarrabun' to show that nearness means closeness in station.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: how does this refutation uphold tanzih (transcendence)?
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

And as for what they held onto ninthly, which are the verses that include raf' (raising), like His statement 'Rather, Allah raised him up to Himself' (4:158), and His statement 'and righteous deed raises it' (35:10). The answer is: Allah, when He raised him to a place of honor and another station, it is permissible metaphorically to say that Allah raised him to Himself. Just as a king, when he elevates a person's rank, it is fitting to say that he raised him from that rank to a high rank and brought him near to himself. Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The opponent cites Quranic verses where things are said to be 'raised' to God (e.g., Jesus raised to Allah, good deeds raised). They argue this implies physical ascent and hence direction. The response explains that 'raf'' here is metaphorical for elevation in honor and rank, not spatial movement. The author draws an analogy with a king who raises a subject's rank: the language of 'raising' and 'bringing near' is used figuratively. The example of 'al-muqarrabun' (those brought near) in the Quran confirms that nearness means closeness in station, not physical proximity. Thus, the verses do not support a spatial direction for God.

The Tenth Quranic Argument Refuted: The Meaning of 'Indiyya
Imam Razi refutes the tenth Quranic proof for direction based on verses using 'ind' (with/at) for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi reinterpreted Quranic verses using 'ind' (with/at) for God as signifying honorific nearness rather than spatial location.

Core Idea
'With' God means honor, not place.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses the strongest Quranic proof for direction: verses that say something is 'with Allah' (عند الله). At first glance, these seem to imply a spatial relationship. But Razi shows that the same language is used for God's nearness to broken hearts and for angels—clearly not spatial. Through prophetic hadith and Quranic examples, he demonstrates that 'indiyya means proximity in rank, not location.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's proof
Notice that the opponent uses Quranic verses with the word 'ind' to argue that Allah has a direction. Read the passage to see how Razi states this.
The word العندية refers to the concept of 'being with'.
2
Understand Razi's response
Razi proposes that 'indiyya means honorific nearness (العندية بالشرف). He provides two pieces of evidence: a hadith about broken hearts and one about God being with His servant's expectation.
These hadith show non-spatial presence.
3
Examine the Quranic example
He then cites verse 38:40 about a high rank (زلفى) to show that 'with Us' can mean honor, not location.
This parallel strengthens the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
All Quranic verses that seem to imply direction are thus interpreted metaphorically. The lesson ends Razi's responses to Quranic proofs.
This clears the way for divine transcendence.

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent uses Quranic expressions like 'ind Allah (with Allah) to argue that God has a spatial location. The response argues that 'indiyya (being 'with') means proximity in honor (al-‘indiyya bi al-sharaf). Evidence includes prophetic hadith: 'I am with the broken-hearted' and 'I am with my servant's expectation', which clearly indicate non-spatial presence. Similarly, the angels are said to be 'with Allah', yet they are not spatially with Him. Furthermore, the Quranic phrase 'la-hu ‘indanā la-zulfā' means a high rank, not physical nearness. Therefore, all such verses are interpreted metaphorically as closeness in station. This concludes the responses to Quranic arguments for direction.

Interpreting the Hadith of the Throne
Imam Razi responds to a hadith about the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi reconciles a hadith about the Throne with the transcendence of God by offering two non-literal interpretations.

Core Idea
Even hadith that seem anthropomorphic can be interpreted to uphold God's transcendence.

Imam Razi now tackles a hadith that opponents of divine transcendence often cite to suggest that God has a direction (jiha). He presents two ways to understand these words, both of which avoid any literal reading that would imply physicality or limitation. The first comes from a variant narration, the second from a metaphorical explanation by a renowned scholar.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its argumentative flow. Notice where the two interpretations begin.
The text is cut off at the end, but the key points are clear.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two ways Imam Razi explains the hadith: one via a different narration, the other via metaphorical interpretation by al-Khattabi.
3
Understand the purpose of metaphorical language
See how al-Khattabi explains that the hadith uses approximating language to help humans grasp God's greatness, not to describe His essence literally.
The creaking of the throne is likened to a saddle under a heavy rider to illustrate awe.
4
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: Why is it important to have these interpretive tools? How does this protect the principle of tanzih (transcendence)?

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأخبار التي تمسكوا بها . فنقول : أما الخبر الأول : فاعلم أن من الماس من روى هذا الخبر على وجه آخر . فقال إنه عليه السلام قال : دوضع عرشه على السموات . هكذا ، وقبب بإصبعه مثل القبة ، فإن حملنا الرواية على هذا الوجه ، فلا إشكال فيه البتة ) وإما أن أخذنا بتلك الرواية . فقال الشيخ أبو سلمان الخطابي : إن الكيفية عن الله تعالى ، وعن صفاته : منتفية (١٠٤) والمقصود من هدا الكلام : التقريب والتفهيم وشرح عظمة الله تعالى من حيث يدركه فهم الإنسان (١٠٥) . وقوله : ( وإنه ينط به ، معناه إنه ليعجز عن جلاله وعظمته ، حتى ينط به ، إذا كان مغلو با (١٠٦) وذلك لأن أطيط الرحل بالراكب إنما يكون لقوة مافوقه ، ولعجزه عن احتماله فهو عليه السلام يقرب (۷). ١٠) بهذا النوع ) من التمسك عنده : معنى عظمته تعالى (۱۰۸) وارتفاع عرشه ، ليعلم المخاطب أنه تعالى أجل وأعلى من أن يجعل شبيها لأحد من خلقه . وأقول : إن ظاهر هذا الحديث يدل على كونه جمل متناهيا ( في
And as for the traditions (akhbar) they held onto, we say: As for the first tradition, know that among the people are those who narrated this tradition in another way. They said that he, peace be upon him, said: 'He placed His throne upon the heavens'—like this—and he gestured with his finger like a dome. If we take the narration in this way, there is no problem at all. And if we take that other narration, then Shaykh Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi said: 'The modality (kayfiyya) of Allah and His attributes is negated, and the intended meaning of this speech is to bring near (taqrib), make understand, and explain the greatness of Allah in a way that human understanding can grasp.' And his saying: 'And indeed it creaks with him' means that it is incapable (of bearing) His majesty and greatness until it creaks with Him, as it is overcome. This is because the creaking of the saddle under the rider only occurs due to the strength of what is above it and its inability to bear it. So he, peace be upon him, by this type of expression intends the meaning of His exaltedness and the elevation of His throne, so that the addressee may know that He is too great and high to be made similar to any of His creation. And I say: The apparent meaning of this tradition indicates that it is a body limited (?) [text cut off].
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning a hadith sometimes used to support direction. The author presents two interpretations. First, some narrate the hadith as 'He placed His throne upon the heavens' with a hand gesture; taken thus, it causes no problem (i.e., it is metaphorical). Second, if we accept the other well-known narration, then al-Khattabi's explanation applies: the hadith is a figurative approximation (taqrib) to convey God's greatness, not a literal description. The phrase 'the throne creaks' is explained as the throne being overwhelmed by God's majesty, analogous to a saddle creaking under a heavy rider. The purpose is to inspire awe and declare God's transcendence above any resemblance to creation. The author then notes that the apparent meaning of the hadith might suggest a limited body, but this is likely cut off in the text, indicating a continuation. The lesson shows how even problematic hadith can be interpreted in ways consistent with divine transcendence.

Correction: Imam Razi shows that the hadith can be interpreted either via a variant narration or as an approximation for human understanding, preserving God's transcendence.

Refuting the Hadith of the Throne's Creaking
Imam Razi refutes the hadith about the throne creaking.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the hadith about the Throne's creaking must be interpreted figuratively to avoid attributing impossible attributes to Allah.

Core Idea
Literal meaning of the hadith would imply Allah is limited — so it must be figurative.

Imam Razi engages with a problematic hadith that some used to claim Allah has a direction or body. He first presents an alternative narration that avoids the issue, then cites a scholar's metaphorical explanation, and finally demonstrates that the literal reading leads to impossible attributes. This forces the conclusion that the hadith requires figurative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative narration
Notice the first way to avoid the problem: some report the Prophet gestured like a dome. If adopted, there is no difficulty.
2
Understand al-Khattabi's allegorical approach
Al-Khattabi explains the creaking as a figure of speech for divine greatness, not a literal sound. The modality (kayfiyya) of Allah is negated.
3
Examine Razi's logical objection
Razi shows that the literal meaning implies a finite body and dependence on the Throne, both impossible for Allah.
4
Conclude the necessity of figurative interpretation
Since the literal meaning contradicts divinity, the hadith must be interpreted figuratively (ta'wil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما تمسكوا بها من القرآن في إثبات الجهة الله تعالى. وبالله التوفيق. وأما الأخبار التي تمسكوا بها. فنقول: أما الخبر الأول: فاعلم أن من الناس من روى هذا الخبر على وجه آخر. فقال إنه عليه السلام قال: وضع عرشه على السموات. هكذا، وقبب بإصبعه مثل القبة. فإن حملنا الرواية على هذا الوجه، فلا إشكال فيه البتة. وإما أن أخذنا بتلك الرواية. فقال الشيخ أبو سلمان الخطابي: إن الكيفية عن الله تعالى، وعن صفاته: منتفية. والمقصود من هذا الكلام: التقريب والتفهيم وشرح عظمة الله تعالى من حيث يدركه فهم الإنسان. وقوله: وإنه ينط به، معناه إنه ليعجز عن جلاله وعظمته، حتى ينط به، إذا كان مغلوبا. وذلك لأن أطيط الرحل بالراكب إنما يكون لقوة ما فوقه، ولعجزه عن احتماله فهو عليه السلام يقرب بهذا النوع من التمسك عنده: معنى عظمته تعالى وارتفاع عرشه، ليعلم المخاطب أنه تعالى أجل وأعلى من أن يجعل شبيها لأحد من خلقه. وأقول: إن ظاهر هذا الحديث يدل على كونه جسما متناهيا في القوة، وإلا لما ينسب بالقوة. وعلى كونه معتمدا على عرشه محتاجا إليه، وإلا لما حصل الأطيط. وكل ذلك ينافي الألهية. فعلمنا: أنه لا بد فيه من حمل اللفظ على غير ظاهره.
He already answered the arguments from the Qur'an for establishing direction for Allah, may He be exalted. And with Allah is success. As for the reports (akhbar) with which they argued, we say: As for the first report, know that among the people are those who narrated this report in another way. They said that he, peace be upon him, said: 'He placed His Throne on the heavens. Like this,' and he gestured with his finger like a dome. So if we adopt the narration in this manner, there is no difficulty in it at all. But if we take that other narration, then Shaykh Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi said: 'The modality (kayfiyya) concerning Allah, and concerning His attributes, is negated. The intention of this speech is approximation, comprehension, and explanation of the greatness of Allah from the perspective that a human's understanding can grasp it.' And his saying, 'and it creaks with him,' means that it is incapable due to His majesty and greatness, so it creaks with him when he is overwhelmed. This is because the creaking of the saddle with the rider only occurs due to the strength of what is above it and its inability to bear it. So he, peace be upon him, brings near through this kind of attachment the meaning of His greatness, may He be exalted, and the loftiness of His Throne, so that the addressee may know that He, exalted is He, is too majestic and lofty to be made similar to any of His creation.' And I say: The apparent meaning of this hadith indicates that He is a finite body in strength, otherwise it would not be attributed to strength. It indicates that He is dependent on His Throne, in need of it, otherwise the creaking would not occur. All of that contradicts divinity. So we know: there must be a figurative interpretation of the word.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the first hadith used by those who claim a direction for Allah. Imam Razi begins by mentioning an alternative narration of the hadith that avoids the problem: some report that the Prophet said 'He placed His Throne on the heavens' and gestured with his finger like a dome—if taken that way, it indicates no difficulty. If we instead adopt the narration mentioning 'creaking' (أطيط), Imam Razi quotes Abu Sulayman al-Khattabi's metaphorical interpretation: that the creaking of the Throne is a figure of speech to convey Allah's greatness and transcendence, not a literal creaking. Al-Khattabi argues that the modality (كيفية) of Allah is negated, and the purpose is approximation for human understanding. Imam Razi then adds his own objection: the literal apparent meaning of the hadith, if taken without figurative interpretation, would imply that Allah is a body of finite strength (since creaking suggests weakness of the bearer) and that He is dependent on His Throne (needing it). Both implications are impossible for Allah and contradict His divinity. Therefore, the hadith must be interpreted figuratively (حمل اللفظ على غير ظاهره).

العرش
الكيفية
التقريب والتفهيم
حمل اللفظ على غير ظاهره

Misconception: The hadith about the Throne creaking must be understood literally as describing a physical creaking sound.
Correction: Imam Razi demonstrates that the literal meaning implies impossible attributes for Allah (finite body, dependence), so it must be interpreted figuratively, as scholars like al-Khattabi explained.

Key Takeaway

Interpreting 'Cinda' in the Hadith of the Book Above the Throne
Imam Razi refutes the second hadith by invoking his earlier interpretation of 'cinda'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the hadith of the book above the Throne by interpreting the word 'cinda' as indicating rank and honor rather than physical location.

Core Idea
‘Cinda’ can mean rank, not just physical nearness.

The second hadith opponents cite says that Allah's book is 'with Him (cinda) above the Throne,' seemingly implying a place. Imam Razi addresses this by referring to his earlier explanation of the word 'cinda' in the Qur'an. In this lesson, we examine this refutation and the key linguistic insight behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that Razi gives a very concise answer, referring to earlier discussion.
2
Understand the linguistic principle
Recall that 'cinda' can mean honor and rank, not physical location. This is key to the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر الثاني: وهو قوله عليه السلام: لما قضى الله الخلق كتب كتابا فهو عنده فوق العرش، فالجواب عنه: ما تقدم من لفظ «عند» في القرآن. وأما الخبر الثالث: فجوابه: إن لفظ «أين» كما يجعل سؤالا عن المكان فقد يجعل سؤالا عن المنزلة والدرجة يقال: أين فلان من فلان؟ فلما السؤال كان عن المنزلة، وإشارتها إلى السماء أي هو رفيع القدر جدا. وإنما اكتفى منها بتلك الإشارة لقصور عقلها، وقلة فهمها. وهذا الجواب يصلح أن يكون جوابا عن تمسكهم بالخبر الثاني، لأن لفظ «عند» يذكر البيان المنزلة والدرجة.
As for the second report: It is his saying, peace be upon him: 'When Allah decreed the creation, He wrote a book, and it is with Him (cinda) above the Throne.' The answer to it is what preceded concerning the word 'cinda' in the Qur'an. As for the third report: Its answer is that the word 'where' (ayna), just as it can be a question about place, can also be a question about rank and degree. It is said: 'Where (ayna) is so-and-so relative to so-and-so?' Since the question was about rank, and her pointing to the sky means he is extremely high in rank. He only sufficed with that gesture due to the deficiency of her intellect and her lack of understanding. This answer can also serve as an answer to their argument from the second report, because the word 'cinda' indicates the expression of rank and degree.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage answers two hadith that are often cited to affirm a physical direction for Allah. The second hadith says that Allah wrote a book that is 'with Him' (عنده) above the Throne. Imam Razi refers back to his earlier discussion of the word 'عند' in the Qur'an, where he argued that it can mean rank or special honor, not physical proximity. The third hadith is the well-known story of the slave girl whom the Prophet asked 'Where is Allah?' and she pointed to the sky; the Prophet said she is a believer. Razi explains that the word 'أين' (where) can be used to ask about rank and degree, not only location. For example, one might say 'Where is so-and-so compared to so-and-so?' meaning 'What is his rank?'. So the slave girl's pointing to the sky indicated Allah's exalted rank, not that He is physically in the sky. The Prophet's approval was of her correct understanding of divine transcendence, not of spatial location. Later, Razi notes that this same answer also addresses the second hadith, because the word 'عند' similarly can indicate rank and degree. Thus, both hadith are properly interpreted without affirming direction.

Key Terms
عند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'cinda above the Throne' implies Allah is in a specific direction above the Throne.
Correction: Razi shows that 'cinda' can mean rank and honor, not physical location.

Checkpoint
What is Imam Razi's answer to the hadith about the book being 'cinda' above the Throne?
A) He rejects the hadith as inauthentic
B) He refers to his earlier interpretation of 'cinda' in the Qur'an
C) He says the book is actually below the Throne
D) He says the hadith is metaphorical only for the Throne

Key Takeaway
The word 'cinda' can indicate rank and honor, not physical location, so the hadith does not imply Allah is in a place.

Imam Razi refutes the third hadith by reinterpreting 'ayna' as a question of rank.

Refuting the Hadith of 'Where Was Our Lord?'
Imam Razi answers the hadith about Allah's existence before creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith about 'amā' to negate any spatial location for Allah before creation.

Core Idea
The hadith of 'amā' actually denies spatial location, not affirms it.

Imam Razi addresses a famous hadith often used to suggest Allah had a location before creation. By analyzing the word 'amā' and its two possible readings, he shows that the hadith actually negates any spatial surroundings, affirming Allah's transcendence. This lesson unpacks Razi's argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its two narrations
Carefully read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that the Prophet's answer includes the word 'amā' with two possible pronunciations: with madd (elongation) and with qasr (shortening).
These two readings lead to different interpretations.
2
Understand the two interpretations
The first reading (madd) means thin cloud. The second (qasr) means non-existence or emptiness. Identify which interpretation aligns with negating spatial location.
Razi emphasizes that the qasr reading is key to understanding the hadith as denying spatial dimensions.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Razi concludes that the hadith means Allah was alone with no above, below, north, or south. This refutes any notion of a physical location for Allah before creation.
The hadith's context is to affirm Allah's transcendence, not to specify a place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الباب أيضاً: أن رجلا قال للنبي: أين كان ربنا قبل أن يخلق السماء؟ فقال عليه السلام: «في عماء تحته هواء، وفوقه هواء، وهذا يروى على وجهين: أحدهما: بالمد وهو السحاب الرقيق. والثاني: بالقصر. فإذا روى مقصورا كان المعنى: إنه تعالى كان وحده ولم يكن معه غيره. شبه العدم بالعمى. فكأنه قال: لم يكن شيء سواه، لا فوق ولا تحت ولا شمال ولا يمين. فإذا قيل: كان في
And from this category also: That a man said to the Prophet: 'Where was our Lord before He created the heaven?' He said, peace be upon him: 'In an 'amā' (عماء) below which is air and above which is air.' This is narrated in two ways: One: with elongation (madd) meaning thin cloud. The second: with shortening (qasr). If narrated with shortening, the meaning is: He, exalted, was alone and nothing else was with Him. He compared non-existence to blindness (or: to 'amā'). It is as if he said: There was nothing besides Him, no above, no below, no north, no south. So if it is said: He was in...
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage addresses another hadith often used to suggest a spatial location for Allah before creation. When asked where Allah was, the Prophet replied that He was in an 'عماء' (amā'), which has two possible interpretations. The first reading (with a long vowel) means 'thin cloud' (سحاب رقيق)—but the hadith specifies there is air above and below, so this is not a literal cloud but a metaphorical description. The second reading (with a short vowel) means 'nothingness' or 'emptiness', and then the Prophet compared non-existence to blindness (or perhaps to 'عماء' meaning blind). The intended meaning is that nothing existed besides Allah—no spatial dimensions like above, below, north, or south. So the hadith actually negates any spatial location for Allah, affirming His transcendence. The text is cut off before completing the answer, but the argument is clear: the different narrations all indicate that locative terms are not applicable to Allah's essence.

Key Terms
عماء
المد والقصر
السحاب الرقيق
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith about 'amā' indicates a specific place (e.g., a cloud) where Allah dwelled before creation.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the hadith, especially with the qasr reading, actually negates any spatial surroundings and affirms that Allah alone existed without any 'where'.

Checkpoint

A) Allah was in a thin cloud
B) Allah was in a void with air above and below
C) Allah was alone with no other thing, and spatial terms like 'above' did not apply
D) Allah was in the heavens before creation

Key Takeaway
The hadith of 'amā', interpreted correctly, affirms Allah's transcendence by denying any spatial location or surroundings before creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Prophet's answer in this hadith redirects the questioner from asking 'where' to recognizing Allah's absolute singularity. Necessity of Ta'wil for the Throne Creaking Hadith
Imam al-Razi argues from the throne creaking hadith for metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's rational argument for interpreting the throne creaking hadith metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
A literal throne creaking would imply Allah's neediness, which is impossible.

Imam al-Razi presents a concise yet powerful argument that the hadith about the throne creaking cannot be taken literally. If it were literal, it would entail that Allah is dependent on His throne, which contradicts His absolute self-sufficiency. Therefore, the wording must be reinterpreted metaphorically. This lesson unpacks that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the logical structure: 'If literal, then dependency; if dependency, then creaking; but creaking contradicts divinity; so ta'wil is necessary.'
2
Trace the logical argument
Identify each premise and the conclusion. Ask yourself why each step is necessary for the argument.
The argument is conditional: if A then B, if B then C, but C is impossible, so not A.
3
Understand the principle of ta'wil
Reflect on the broader principle: when the literal meaning leads to an impossible consequence for Allah, metaphorical interpretation becomes obligatory.
This is a key principle in kalam for handling problematic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلا لما [؟] ينسبة بالقوة وعلى كونه معتمدا على عرشه محتاجا إليه، وإلا لما حصل الأطيط. وكل ذلك ينافي الألهية. فعلمنا: أنه لا بد فيه من حمل اللفظ على غير ظاهره.
And otherwise [??] he attributes it with power and that He is depending on His throne, in need of it, and otherwise the creaking would not occur. And all that contradicts divinity. So we know: it is necessary in it to interpret the wording in other than its apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is part of Imam al-Razi's refutation of anthropomorphism based on the hadith that the throne creaked. He argues that if Allah were physically sitting on the throne, He would be dependent on it, leading to neediness, and then the throne would creak (as reported). All of this is incompatible with divinity. Therefore, the apparent meaning must be reinterpreted metaphorically. This establishes a key principle of ta'wil for problematic hadith.

Key Terms
الأطيط
الألهية
حمل اللفظ على غير ظاهره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the hadith about the throne creaking proves that Allah literally sits on the throne and that the throne physically creaks under His weight.
Correction: Imam al-Razi shows that such a literal interpretation leads to contradictions with Allah's divinity (neediness, physical effects), so the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically.

Checkpoint

A) The throne is weak
B) Allah is sitting on the throne
C) The throne is speaking
D) The angels are moving

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi demonstrates that literal interpretation of the throne creaking hadith leads to contradictions with Allah's divinity, necessitating metaphorical interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The word 'inda in divine texts indicates rank, not location.

This concise passage demonstrates the consistency of his hermeneutical method.

3
Extract the interpretive principle

This principle guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih).

Interpreting 'Where' in the Slave Girl Hadith
Al-Razi explains 'where' as a question of status in the slave girl hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the word 'where' in the slave girl hadith refers to status and rank, not physical location, preserving Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
‎'Where' can ask about rank, not just place.

Imam al-Razi addresses a key objection from those who claim the hadith of the slave girl pointing to the sky proves Allah is in a physical direction. He explains that the Arabic word 'where' (أين) has a broader usage: it can inquire about status and degree. This interpretation defends divine transcendence while respecting the Prophet's acceptance of her answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting how al-Razi distinguishes between place and status in the word 'أين'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'يقال: أين فلان من فلان؟' as an example.
2
Understand the key linguistic point
Recognize that 'أين' can be used to ask about relative rank, as in 'Where is so-and-so compared to so-and-so?' meaning 'What is his status?'
3
Apply the explanation to the hadith
See how al-Razi applies this to the slave girl: the question was about Allah's status, so her pointing to the sky signifies His high rank, not a physical location.
4
Note the reason for the Prophet's acceptance
Al-Razi explains that the Prophet accepted her gesture due to her limited intellect and lack of understanding, not because the answer was literal.
This highlights the principle of addressing people according to their capacity.
5
Connect to the second hadith (the word 'عند')
Al-Razi notes that this same interpretation applies to the word 'عند' (when/near) in the second report, showing a consistent method of interpreting ambiguous terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخبر الثالث: فجوابه: إن لفظ 'أين' كما يجعل سؤالا عن المكان فقد يجعل سؤالا عن المنزلة والدرجة، يقال: أين فلان من فلان؟ فلما السؤال كان عن المنزلة، وإشارتها إلى السماء. أي هو رفيع القدر جدا. وإنما اكتفى منها بتلك الإشارة لقصور عقلها، وقلة فهمها. وهذا الجواب يصلح أن يكون جوابا عن تمسكهم بالخبر الثاني، وهو لفظ 'عنده'، لأن لفظ 'عند' يذكر لبيان المنزلة والدرجة.
As for the third report: its answer: The word 'where?' just as it can be a question about place, it can also be a question about status and rank. It is said: 'Where is so-and-so relative to so-and-so?' So since the question was about status, and her pointing to the sky means he is extremely high in rank. And he (peace be upon him) sufficed with that gesture from her due to the limitation of her intellect and her lack of understanding. And this answer suffices as an answer for their reliance on the second report, namely the word 'when', because the word 'when' is mentioned to indicate status and rank.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage interprets the well-known hadith where the Prophet asked a slave girl 'Where is Allah?' and she pointed to the sky. Imam al-Razi argues that the word 'where' can ask about status, not just location. He supports this by noting the Arabic usage 'Where is so-and-so relative to so-and-so?' meaning rank. Since the girl's question was about status, her pointing to the sky indicates Allah's extreme highness in rank, not physical direction. The Prophet accepted her answer because she lacked intellectual capacity for deeper explanation. Al-Razi then notes that this same interpretation applies to the second hadith about 'when', showing consistency in handling ambiguous terms.

The Hadith of 'Ama' and Its Two Readings
Imam al-Razi analyzes two readings of 'ama' in a hadith and argues for the short-vowel reading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi prefers the short-vowel reading of 'ama' and how it affirms Allah's transcendence before creation.

Core Idea
The word 'ama' in a hadith does not mean clouds; it affirms nothingness beside Allah.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi examines a famous hadith where the Prophet describes Allah's state before creation. The key word 'ama' has two possible readings, and al-Razi argues for the one that best upholds divine transcendence. We'll follow his reasoning and see why the short-vowel reading (ama) is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its two readings
Understand what the man asked and the Prophet's response. Note the two vowel lengths mentioned.
The hadith is quoted with the word 'ama' in the original.
2
Understand the meaning of each reading
The long-vowel reading (madd) means thin clouds. The short-vowel (qasr) means blindness or nothingness.
Al-Razi explains the short-vowel reading as a metaphor for non-existence.
3
See al-Razi's preference and evidence
He prefers the short-vowel reading, supported by another hadith: 'Allah was and nothing was with Him.'
This hadith from 'Imran ibn Husayn is used as evidence.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This upholds that before creation, Allah existed alone without any spatial or physical attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الباب أيضاً: أن رجلا قال للنبي: أين كان ربنا قبل أن يخلق السماء؟ فقال عليه السلام: 'في عماء تحته هواء، وفوقه هواء'. وهذا يروى على وجهين: أحدهما: بالمد وهو السحاب الرقيق. والثاني: بالقصر. فإذا روي مقصورا كان المعنى: إنه تعالى كان وحده ولم يكن معه غيره. شبه العدم بالعمى. فكأنه قال: لم يكن شيء سواه، لا فوق ولا تحت ولا شمال ولا يمين. فإذا قيل: كان في عمى، فالمعمى أنه تعالى كان فيه بمعنى القدرة والتدبير. والرواية الأولى: أولى. لما روي عن عمران بن الحصين قال: قال رجل لرسول الله: أخبرنا عن أول هذا الأمر؟ قال: 'كان الله ولم يكن معه شيء'. وهذا يدل على أن رواية العمى - بالقصر - أولى من المد.
And from this category also: that a man said to the Prophet: 'Where was our Lord before He created the heaven?' So he said, peace be upon him: 'In an 'ama', beneath which is air, and above which is air.' And this is narrated in two ways: one with madd (long vowel) which means thin clouds, and the second with qasr (short vowel). If narrated with qasr, the meaning is: He, exalted, was alone and nothing else with Him. He likened non-existence to blindness (ama). So it is as if he said: There was nothing besides Him, no above, no below, no left, no right. So if it is said: He was in ama, the meaning is that He, exalted, was in it in the sense of power and governance. And the first narration (with qasr) is preferable. Because it is narrated from 'Imran ibn Husayn that a man said to the Messenger of Allah: 'Tell us about the beginning of this affair.' He said: 'Allah was and nothing was with Him.' And this indicates that the narration of ama with qasr is preferable to the one with madd.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage deals with a hadith where the Prophet describes Allah's existence before creation using the word 'ama. Imam al-Razi explains that 'ama has two readings: with a long vowel meaning thin clouds, and with a short vowel meaning blindness (nothingness). He argues for the short-vowel reading because it supports the meaning that Allah alone existed with nothing else, no spatial dimensions. He supports this preference with the hadith from 'Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet said 'Allah was and nothing was with him.' This shows that the correct interpretation denies any physical or spatial attributes before creation, affirming Allah's transcendence.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Razi shows the short-vowel reading means nothingness, which better fits the context of Allah's existence before creation.

Checkpoint

D) Neither reading is reliable

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi prefers the short-vowel reading of 'ama' because it means 'nothingness,' affirming that before creation, Allah alone existed with no spatial or physical attributes.

The Angel Hadith: Allah is Everywhere by Governance
Imam al-Razi interprets the angel hadith as showing Allah's omnipresence through governance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi interprets a hadith where angels give different locations for Allah to show that Allah is everywhere through His preservation and governance, not physically.

Core Idea
Allah is everywhere by His preservation and governance, not by physical presence.

This lesson examines a hadith where angels give conflicting answers about where Allah is. Imam al-Razi, following the Qur'anic verses, explains that Allah is in every place through His attributes of preservation, governance, and divinity, not through spatial location. Understanding this is crucial for the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Start by reading the Arabic hadith and its literal translation. Notice the different answers given by the angels.
The hadith mentions earths, heavens, east, and west.
2
Identify the three Qur'anic verses
Al-Razi cites three verses to support his interpretation. Find each verse in the passage and see how they relate to the hadith.
The verses emphasize that Allah is God in heavens and earth, and that His face is everywhere.
3
Understand the interpretation
Al-Razi concludes that Allah is in every place by His preservation (hifz), governance (tadbir), and divinity (ilahiyya). Reflect on how this resolves the apparent contradiction.
This interpretation upholds Allah's transcendence while affirming His omnipresence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هذا الباب أيضا: ما روي أنس -رضي الله عنه قال: كان جبريل عند النبي وملك. فقال: أين تركت ربنا؟ فقال: في الأرضين. فجاء آخر. فقال: أين تركت ربنا؟ فقال: في سبع سموات. فجاءه آخر فسأله عنه؟ فقال: في المشرق. وآخر في المغرب. والتأويل: إنه على وفق قوله تعالى: 'وهو الذي في السماء إله وفي الأرض إله'، وقوله: 'وهو الله في السموات وفي الأرض'، وقوله: 'فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله'، أي هو تعالى في كل مكان بالحفظ والتدبير والإلهية. وأما قصة المعراج: فالمقصود: أنه يريه الله تعالى أنواع مخلوقاته في العالم العلوي والعالم السفلي، لتكون مشاهداته للدلائل أكثر، فتكون نفس أقوى وأكمل. كما في حق الخليل عليه السلام. وأما قوله: 'ثم دنا فتدلى، فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى'، ففيه وجوه:
And from this category also: what was narrated from Anas - may Allah be pleased with him - he said: Jibril was with the Prophet and an angel. He said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the earths.' Then another came. He said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the seven heavens.' Then another came and asked him about it? He said: 'In the east.' And another in the west. And the interpretation: It is in accordance with His saying, exalted: 'And He is the One who is in the heaven a god and on the earth a god' and His saying: 'And He is Allah in the heavens and on the earth' and His saying: 'Wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' that is, He, exalted, is in every place by preservation, governance, and divinity. As for the story of the Mi'raj: the purpose is that Allah, exalted, shows him types of His creations in the higher world and the lower world, so that his observations of evidences become more numerous, and thus his soul becomes stronger and more perfect. As in the case of al-Khalil (Abraham), peace be upon him. As for His saying: 'Then he drew near and descended, and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer', it has aspects:

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith where an angel asks Jibril where he left Allah, and multiple angels give different answers (earths, heavens, east, west). Imam al-Razi interprets this by referencing Qur'anic verses that indicate Allah is everywhere in terms of His preservation, governance, and divinity, not physically. Then he addresses the Mi'raj (Ascension) of the Prophet, explaining that its purpose was to show the Prophet various creations in the heavens and earth to increase his evidence-based knowledge and strengthen his soul, similar to how Allah showed Abraham the heavens. He begins to comment on the verse 'Then he drew near and descended...' but the text cuts off, indicating there are more aspects to discuss.

The Purpose of the Prophet's Mi'raj
Imam al-Razi explains the Mi'raj's purpose as increasing the Prophet's knowledge and soul strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's explanation that the Mi'raj was to show the Prophet divine signs to increase his knowledge and strengthen his soul.

Core Idea
The Mi'raj increased the Prophet's evidences and soul strength.

The Mi'raj (Ascension) of the Prophet Muhammad is often understood as a physical journey. Imam al-Razi, however, focuses on its spiritual purpose: to provide the Prophet with direct observations of Allah's creations, enhancing his certainty and spiritual perfection, similar to what Allah showed Prophet Abraham.

Guided Path

Read the passage on Mi'raj

The passage mentions both the higher and lower worlds.
2
Compare with the case of Abraham

Abraham was shown the heavens and earth to increase his certainty.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why witnessing more signs leads to a stronger soul. How does this relate to the Prophet's role?

The Meaning of 'Allah Existed and Nothing Was With Him'
Razi refutes anthropomorphism by interpreting a hadith about Allah's existence before creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him' as referring to Allah's power and planning, not a state of spatial emptiness.

Core Idea
Allah's 'being alone' means His eternal power, not physical isolation.

The hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him' might seem to imply that Allah was once in a state of being physically alone. But Imam Razi explains that this refers to His attribute of power and planning, not spatial emptiness. Let's see how he argues this using another narration.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first part of the passage where Razi discusses the meaning of 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him'. Notice the reference to another narration.
The Arabic contains some unclear words; focus on the meaning.
2
Understand the two narrations
Razi mentions two versions of the hadith: one with 'al-ama' (the blind) short form and one long form. He argues the short form is more appropriate.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
Understand that the point is to avoid implying that Allah was in a void or that space existed alongside Him. The interpretation preserves His transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
همی. فالمعنى (١١٢) أنه تعالى كان فيه بمعنى القدرة والتدبير . والرواية الأولى : أولى . لما روى عن عمران بن الحصين (١١٣) قال : قال رجل لرسول الله (١١٤) : أخبرنا عن أول هذا الأمر ؟ قال : وكان الله ولم يكن معه شيء، وهذا يدل على أن رواية العمى - بالقصر - : أولى من المد. ومن هذا الباب أيضا : ماروى أنس -رضي الله عنه قال : كان جبريل عند النبي فجاء ملك . فقال : أين تركت ربنا ؟ فقال : في الأرضين فجاء آخر . فقال : أين تركت ربنا ؟ فقال : في سبع سموات فجاءه آخر. فسأله عنه ؟ فقال : فى المشرق . وآخر فى المغرب . والتأويل : إنه على وفق قوله تعالى : « وهو الذى فى السماء إله ، وفى الأرض إله» (١١٥) ، وقوله : « وهو الله فى السموات وفى الأرض» (١١٦) ، وقوله : ( فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله» (١١٧) ، أى هو تعالى في كل مكان بالحفظ والتدبير والإلهية

و أما قصة المعراج . فالمقصود : أنه يريه الله - تعالى - أنواع مخلوقاته في العالم العلوي والعالم السفلى ، لتكون مشاهداته للدلائل أكثر ، فتصير نفسه أقوى وأكمل . كما في حق الخليل - عليه السلام - وأما قوله : . ثم دنا فتدلى . فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى (١١٨) ، ففيه وجوه :

الأول : إن هذا الدنو دنو المنزلة والكرامة . كقوله تعالى: « واسجد واقترب» (١١٩) ، وقال عليه السلام - حكاية عن الله تعالى : تقرب إلى شبراً تقربت إليه ذراعا ،

الثاني : . ثم دنا فتدلى ، أى جبريل دنا من محمد - عليهما السلام - والدليل عليه : قوله تعالى في آية أخرى : ( ولقد رآه بالأفق المبين (١٢٠)) ثم لما دنا جبريل من محمد عليهما السلام - حصل الوحي من الله تعالى إليه ولهذا قال (١٢١): ( فأوحى إلى عبده ما أوحى (١٢٢) ،

وأما الجواب عن التمسك بقول فرعون « يا هامان ابن لي صرحا» (١٢٢) فهو أن هذا الكلام من فرعون . وهو معارض بأن موسى - عليه السلام - لم : يقل الرب في السماء ، بل قال : د رب السماء (١٢٣) ، ثم إن فرعون كان قد ظن فيه أن الإله مستقر في السماء . فهذا هو الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ) وبالله التوفيق (١٢٤))
? So the meaning (112) is that He, exalted, was in it [i.e., in that state] with the meaning of power and planning. The first narration is more appropriate. Because it is narrated from Imran ibn Husayn (113) who said: A man asked the Messenger of Allah (114): Inform us about the beginning of this matter? He said: 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him.' This indicates that the narration of 'al-ama' (the blind) with the short form is more appropriate than the long form. And of this category also is what Anas - may Allah be pleased with him - narrated: Jibril was with the Prophet, then an angel came and said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the earths.' Then another came and said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the seven heavens.' Then another came and asked him? He said: 'In the east.' And another in the west. The interpretation is that it is in accordance with His statement: 'He is the God in the heaven and God in the earth' (115), and His statement: 'He is Allah in the heavens and in the earth' (116), and His statement: 'Wheresoever you turn, there is the face of Allah' (117), meaning He, exalted, is in every place by preservation, planning, and divinity.

As for the story of the Ascension (Mi'raj): the intent is that Allah - exalted - shows him types of His creatures in the upper world and the lower world, so that his observations of evidence are more numerous, and his soul becomes stronger and more perfect, as in the case of al-Khalil (Abraham) - peace be upon him. And as for His statement: 'Then he drew near and came down, and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer' (118), there are aspects to it:

The first: that this nearness is the nearness of station and honor, like His statement: 'Prostrate and draw near' (119), and the Prophet - peace be upon him - said, narrating from Allah: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by a cubit.'

The second: 'Then he drew near and came down' means Jibril drew near to Muhammad - peace be upon them both - and the evidence for it is His statement in another verse: 'And he certainly saw him on the clear horizon' (120). Then when Jibril drew near to Muhammad - peace be upon them both - the revelation occurred from Allah to him. Therefore He said (121): 'And He inspired to His servant what He inspired' (122).

As for the answer to those who cling to the statement of Pharaoh 'O Haman, build for me a tower' (122): It is that this speech is from Pharaoh. It is countered by the fact that Moses - peace be upon him - did not say 'the Lord in the heaven', but rather said 'the Lord of the heaven' (123). Then Pharaoh had assumed that the deity is settled in heaven. This is the answer to this doubt. And with Allah is success (124).
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih) concerning Allah's location and nearness. The author, Imam al-Razi, interprets various scriptural texts that might imply physical location or proximity, showing they are metaphorical.

First, he addresses the hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him.' He explains that the preferred narration is the short version (without the word 'anything' or similar). He cites the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet says 'Allah existed and nothing was with him.' This means that in that state, Allah was characterized by power and planning, not spatial emptiness.

Second, he discusses the story of the angels asking Jibril where he left Allah, with contradictory answers. Razi interprets this not as Allah being physically in different places, but in accordance with Quranic verses that Allah is in heaven and earth by His divinity, preservation, and planning. He cites verses: 'He is the God in the heaven and God in the earth,' 'He is Allah in the heavens and in the earth,' and 'Wheresoever you turn, there is the face of Allah.' The phrase 'face of Allah' is metaphorical, meaning His direction or attention.

Third, he addresses the Ascension (Mi'raj) and the verse 'Then he drew near and came down' (Quran 53:8-9). He gives two interpretations: either it is the nearness of station and honor, as in the hadith qudsi about drawing near, or it refers to Jibril drawing near to Muhammad to deliver revelation. The second interpretation is supported by another verse about seeing Jibril on the clear horizon.

Finally, he refutes the argument from Pharaoh's statement 'O Haman, build me a tower' to reach the Lord. Razi points out that Pharaoh assumed Allah is stationed in heaven, but Moses said 'Lord of the heaven,' not 'Lord in the heaven.' So Pharaoh's assumption is false, and his question does not prove location.

The passage concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

Razi's method is to interpret ambiguous texts in a way consistent with divine transcendence, avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
التأويل
الرواية العمى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him' means there was a time when Allah was in a void or space.
Correction: Razi interprets it as referring to Allah's power and planning, not spatial emptiness. The term 'nothing' means no other object co-existed, but this does not imply a location.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the correct interpretation of 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him'?
A) Allah was alone in a spatial void
B) Allah was characterized by power and planning
C) Allah was in a state of rest
D) Allah was physically located in heaven

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him' refers to Allah's eternal attributes, not spatial emptiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him,' does your mind immediately form a mental image of a void or empty space? What does that reveal about your tendency to anthropomorphize the Uncreated?
?
If you truly internalized that Allah's 'being alone' refers to His infinite power and not physical aloneness, would your awe of Him grow? How might that change your awareness of His presence right now?
?
Is there a part of your life where you treat Allah as if He were distant or isolated, rather than as the One who holds all power and plans? Will you repent from that neglect today?
