Interpreting Contradictory Reports About Allah's Location
Razi interprets contradictory angelic reports about where Allah is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi reconciles contradictory reports about where Allah is located by interpreting them metaphorically as His preservation and divinity throughout creation.

Core Idea
Allah is everywhere by His power, not physically.

There are conflicting reports from angels about where they left Allah: some say in the earths, others in the heavens, east, west. How can these be reconciled? Razi explains that these refer to Allah's attributes of preservation and divinity, not His physical location.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of the angels
Read the narrative where Jibril is asked where he left Allah and gives different answers. Notice the pattern.
Each answer places Allah in a different location.
2
Understand Razi's interpretation
Razi says these answers are consistent with Quranic verses that describe Allah as God in heaven and earth, and that His 'face' is everywhere. The meaning is that Allah is present everywhere by His preservation, planning, and divinity.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
The lesson here is that Allah is not physically located anywhere; rather, His attributes encompass all places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
همی. فالمعنى (١١٢) أنه تعالى كان فيه بمعنى القدرة والتدبير . والرواية الأولى : أولى . لما روى عن عمران بن الحصين (١١٣) قال : قال رجل لرسول الله (١١٤) : أخبرنا عن أول هذا الأمر ؟ قال : وكان الله ولم يكن معه شيء، وهذا يدل على أن رواية العمى - بالقصر - : أولى من المد. ومن هذا الباب أيضا : ماروى أنس -رضي الله عنه قال : كان جبريل عند النبي فجاء ملك . فقال : أين تركت ربنا ؟ فقال : في الأرضين فجاء آخر . فقال : أين تركت ربنا ؟ فقال : في سبع سموات فجاءه آخر. فسأله عنه ؟ فقال : فى المشرق . وآخر فى المغرب . والتأويل : إنه على وفق قوله تعالى : « وهو الذى فى السماء إله ، وفى الأرض إله» (١١٥) ، وقوله : « وهو الله فى السموات وفى الأرض» (١١٦) ، وقوله : ( فأينما تولوا فثم وجه الله» (١١٧) ، أى هو تعالى في كل مكان بالحفظ والتدبير والإلهية

و أما قصة المعراج . فالمقصود : أنه يريه الله - تعالى - أنواع مخلوقاته في العالم العلوي والعالم السفلى ، لتكون مشاهداته للدلائل أكثر ، فتصير نفسه أقوى وأكمل . كما في حق الخليل - عليه السلام - وأما قوله : . ثم دنا فتدلى . فكان قاب قوسين أو أدنى (١١٨) ، ففيه وجوه :

الأول : إن هذا الدنو دنو المنزلة والكرامة . كقوله تعالى: « واسجد واقترب» (١١٩) ، وقال عليه السلام - حكاية عن الله تعالى : تقرب إلى شبراً تقربت إليه ذراعا ،

الثاني : . ثم دنا فتدلى ، أى جبريل دنا من محمد - عليهما السلام - والدليل عليه : قوله تعالى في آية أخرى : ( ولقد رآه بالأفق المبين (١٢٠)) ثم لما دنا جبريل من محمد عليهما السلام - حصل الوحي من الله تعالى إليه ولهذا قال (١٢١): ( فأوحى إلى عبده ما أوحى (١٢٢) ،

وأما الجواب عن التمسك بقول فرعون « يا هامان ابن لي صرحا» (١٢٢) فهو أن هذا الكلام من فرعون . وهو معارض بأن موسى - عليه السلام - لم : يقل الرب في السماء ، بل قال : د رب السماء (١٢٣) ، ثم إن فرعون كان قد ظن فيه أن الإله مستقر في السماء . فهذا هو الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ) وبالله التوفيق (١٢٤))
? So the meaning (112) is that He, exalted, was in it [i.e., in that state] with the meaning of power and planning. The first narration is more appropriate. Because it is narrated from Imran ibn Husayn (113) who said: A man asked the Messenger of Allah (114): Inform us about the beginning of this matter? He said: 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him.' This indicates that the narration of 'al-ama' (the blind) with the short form is more appropriate than the long form. And of this category also is what Anas - may Allah be pleased with him - narrated: Jibril was with the Prophet, then an angel came and said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the earths.' Then another came and said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the seven heavens.' Then another came and asked him? He said: 'In the east.' And another in the west. The interpretation is that it is in accordance with His statement: 'He is the God in the heaven and God in the earth' (115), and His statement: 'He is Allah in the heavens and in the earth' (116), and His statement: 'Wheresoever you turn, there is the face of Allah' (117), meaning He, exalted, is in every place by preservation, planning, and divinity.

As for the story of the Ascension (Mi'raj): the intent is that Allah - exalted - shows him types of His creatures in the upper world and the lower world, so that his observations of evidence are more numerous, and his soul becomes stronger and more perfect, as in the case of al-Khalil (Abraham) - peace be upon him. And as for His statement: 'Then he drew near and came down, and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer' (118), there are aspects to it:

The first: that this nearness is the nearness of station and honor, like His statement: 'Prostrate and draw near' (119), and the Prophet - peace be upon him - said, narrating from Allah: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by a cubit.'

The second: 'Then he drew near and came down' means Jibril drew near to Muhammad - peace be upon them both - and the evidence for it is His statement in another verse: 'And he certainly saw him on the clear horizon' (120). Then when Jibril drew near to Muhammad - peace be upon them both - the revelation occurred from Allah to him. Therefore He said (121): 'And He inspired to His servant what He inspired' (122).

As for the answer to those who cling to the statement of Pharaoh 'O Haman, build for me a tower' (122): It is that this speech is from Pharaoh. It is countered by the fact that Moses - peace be upon him - did not say 'the Lord in the heaven', but rather said 'the Lord of the heaven' (123). Then Pharaoh had assumed that the deity is settled in heaven. This is the answer to this doubt. And with Allah is success (124).
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih) concerning Allah's location and nearness. The author, Imam al-Razi, interprets various scriptural texts that might imply physical location or proximity, showing they are metaphorical.

First, he addresses the hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him.' He explains that the preferred narration is the short version (without the word 'anything' or similar). He cites the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet says 'Allah existed and nothing was with him.' This means that in that state, Allah was characterized by power and planning, not spatial emptiness.

Second, he discusses the story of the angels asking Jibril where he left Allah, with contradictory answers. Razi interprets this not as Allah being physically in different places, but in accordance with Quranic verses that Allah is in heaven and earth by His divinity, preservation, and planning. He cites verses: 'He is the God in the heaven and God in the earth,' 'He is Allah in the heavens and in the earth,' and 'Wheresoever you turn, there is the face of Allah.' The phrase 'face of Allah' is metaphorical, meaning His direction or attention.

Third, he addresses the Ascension (Mi'raj) and the verse 'Then he drew near and came down' (Quran 53:8-9). He gives two interpretations: either it is the nearness of station and honor, as in the hadith qudsi about drawing near, or it refers to Jibril drawing near to Muhammad to deliver revelation. The second interpretation is supported by another verse about seeing Jibril on the clear horizon.

Finally, he refutes the argument from Pharaoh's statement 'O Haman, build me a tower' to reach the Lord. Razi points out that Pharaoh assumed Allah is stationed in heaven, but Moses said 'Lord of the heaven,' not 'Lord in the heaven.' So Pharaoh's assumption is false, and his question does not prove location.

The passage concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

Razi's method is to interpret ambiguous texts in a way consistent with divine transcendence, avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
التأويل
الوجه
الإلهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the contradictory answers show that Allah is physically located in different places at different times.
Correction: Razi explains that these answers refer to Allah's attributes of preservation and divinity, not physical location.

Checkpoint
How does Razi interpret the contradictory answers about where Allah is?
A) They refer to different physical locations of Allah
B) They are errors in the narration
C) They refer to Allah's preservation, planning, and divinity
D) They are meant to test the angels

Key Takeaway
Contradictory reports about Allah's location are reconciled by understanding them as references to His attributes, not His physical presence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's nearness, do you ever visualize a physical location or direction? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you actively do to retrain your mind?
?
The angels gave four different answers about where Allah is. Is there any area in your life where you have reduced Allah to a limited concept or confined His presence? Commit to one way you will expand your understanding this week.
?
Razi uses metaphorical interpretation to reconcile conflicting reports. Are you willing to accept interpretations that feel uncomfortable because they demand intellectual humility? Name one belief about Allah that you need to re-examine with this approach.

Interpreting the Prophet's Nearness in the Ascension
Razi interprets the verse 'Then he drew near' from the Mi'raj story.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations Imam Razi gives for the verse 'Then he drew near and came down' in the context of the Mi'raj.

Core Idea
The nearness in the Mi'raj is either honor or Jibril's approach.

The Quranic verse about the Prophet drawing near during the Ascension might be interpreted as a physical approach to Allah. Razi offers two alternative interpretations: one refers to nearness in station and honor, the other to Jibril drawing near to Muhammad. Let's examine both.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mi'raj passage
Read the part starting with 'As for the story of the Ascension...'. Notice the two interpretations Razi gives.
2
First interpretation: nearness of honor
Razi cites other texts that speak of drawing near in terms of honor and reward, such as 'Prostrate and draw near' and the hadith qudsi about mutual drawing near.
3
Second interpretation: Jibril's approach
Razi uses another verse about seeing Jibril on the clear horizon to argue that the subject is Jibril drawing near to the Prophet, not Allah.
4
Understand the purpose of Mi'raj
Razi also explains that the Mi'raj was to show the Prophet the wonders of creation, strengthening his soul.

Refuting Pharaoh's Assumption About Allah's Location
Razi refutes Pharaoh's argument for Allah's location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the argument that Pharaoh's request to build a tower proves Allah is located in the heavens.

Core Idea
Pharaoh's mistake was assuming Allah is in the heaven; Moses said Lord of the heaven.

Pharaoh asked Haman to build a tower so he could climb to the Lord of Moses. Does this imply that Allah is physically above? Razi points out that Moses said 'Lord of the heaven', not 'Lord in the heaven', and that Pharaoh's assumption was an error. Let's see how this refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Pharaoh
Read the part where Pharaoh asks for a tower to reach the Lord of Moses. Notice Razi's response.
2
Distinguish between 'in' and 'of'
Razi highlights that Moses said 'Lord of the heaven', which implies sovereignty over creation, not physical location.
3
Understand the refutation
Razi argues that Pharaoh's statement is not authoritative; it is based on a false assumption. The correct understanding is that Allah is not confined to a location.

وأما الجواب عن التمسك بقول فرعون « يا هامان ابن لي صرحا» (١٢٢) فهو أن هذا الكلام من فرعون . وهو معارض بأن موسى - عليه السلام - لم : يقل الرب في السماء ، بل قال : د رب السماء (١٢٣) ، ثم إن فرعون كان قد ظن فيه أن الإله مستقر في السماء . فهذا هو الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ) وبالله التوفيق (١٢٤))
? So the meaning (112) is that He, exalted, was in it [i.e., in that state] with the meaning of power and planning. The first narration is more appropriate. Because it is narrated from Imran ibn Husayn (113) who said: A man asked the Messenger of Allah (114): Inform us about the beginning of this matter? He said: 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him.' This indicates that the narration of 'al-ama' (the blind) with the short form is more appropriate than the long form. And of this category also is what Anas - may Allah be pleased with him - narrated: Jibril was with the Prophet, then an angel came and said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the earths.' Then another came and said: 'Where did you leave our Lord?' He said: 'In the seven heavens.' Then another came and asked him? He said: 'In the east.' And another in the west. The interpretation is that it is in accordance with His statement: 'He is the God in the heaven and God in the earth' (115), and His statement: 'He is Allah in the heavens and in the earth' (116), and His statement: 'Wheresoever you turn, there is the face of Allah' (117), meaning He, exalted, is in every place by preservation, planning, and divinity.

As for the story of the Ascension (Mi'raj): the intent is that Allah - exalted - shows him types of His creatures in the upper world and the lower world, so that his observations of evidence are more numerous, and his soul becomes stronger and more perfect, as in the case of al-Khalil (Abraham) - peace be upon him. And as for His statement: 'Then he drew near and came down, and was at a distance of two bow-lengths or nearer' (118), there are aspects to it:

The first: that this nearness is the nearness of station and honor, like His statement: 'Prostrate and draw near' (119), and the Prophet - peace be upon him - said, narrating from Allah: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by a cubit.'

The second: 'Then he drew near and came down' means Jibril drew near to Muhammad - peace be upon them both - and the evidence for it is His statement in another verse: 'And he certainly saw him on the clear horizon' (120). Then when Jibril drew near to Muhammad - peace be upon them both - the revelation occurred from Allah to him. Therefore He said (121): 'And He inspired to His servant what He inspired' (122).

As for the answer to those who cling to the statement of Pharaoh 'O Haman, build for me a tower' (122): It is that this speech is from Pharaoh. It is countered by the fact that Moses - peace be upon him - did not say 'the Lord in the heaven', but rather said 'the Lord of the heaven' (123). Then Pharaoh had assumed that the deity is settled in heaven. This is the answer to this doubt. And with Allah is success (124).
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih) concerning Allah's location and nearness. The author, Imam al-Razi, interprets various scriptural texts that might imply physical location or proximity, showing they are metaphorical.

First, he addresses the hadith 'Allah existed and nothing was with Him.' He explains that the preferred narration is the short version (without the word 'anything' or similar). He cites the hadith of Imran ibn Husayn where the Prophet says 'Allah existed and nothing was with him.' This means that in that state, Allah was characterized by power and planning, not spatial emptiness.

Second, he discusses the story of the angels asking Jibril where he left Allah, with contradictory answers. Razi interprets this not as Allah being physically in different places, but in accordance with Quranic verses that Allah is in heaven and earth by His divinity, preservation, and planning. He cites verses: 'He is the God in the heaven and God in the earth,' 'He is Allah in the heavens and in the earth,' and 'Wheresoever you turn, there is the face of Allah.' The phrase 'face of Allah' is metaphorical, meaning His direction or attention.

Third, he addresses the Ascension (Mi'raj) and the verse 'Then he drew near and came down' (Quran 53:8-9). He gives two interpretations: either it is the nearness of station and honor, as in the hadith qudsi about drawing near, or it refers to Jibril drawing near to Muhammad to deliver revelation. The second interpretation is supported by another verse about seeing Jibril on the clear horizon.

Finally, he refutes the argument from Pharaoh's statement 'O Haman, build me a tower' to reach the Lord. Razi points out that Pharaoh assumed Allah is stationed in heaven, but Moses said 'Lord of the heaven,' not 'Lord in the heaven.' So Pharaoh's assumption is false, and his question does not prove location.

The passage concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

Razi's method is to interpret ambiguous texts in a way consistent with divine transcendence, avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
شبهة فرعون
السماء
رب السماء

Correction: Razi points out that Moses said 'Lord of the heaven', indicating sovereignty, not location.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the argument from Pharaoh's statement?

B) He says Moses said 'Lord of the heaven' not 'Lord in the heaven'
C) He argues Pharaoh was lying
D) He says the tower was never built

Key Takeaway
Pharaoh's assumption about Allah's location is refuted by Moses' phrase 'Lord of the heaven', which denotes sovereignty, not spatial confinement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is in the heaven,' do you ever unconsciously imagine Him confined to a place, like a throne or the sky? How does Moses' precise wording 'Lord of the heaven' challenge that picture in your heart today?
?
Pharaoh built a tower to 'see' the deity. What 'towers' do you build in your life—expectations, rituals, or mental images—that assume God must show up in a certain way? Will you dismantle them and submit to His transcendence?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is the Sovereign Lord of the heaven, not contained in any location, how would it change the way you pray, seek Him, or trust Him in your daily struggles?

Divine Nearness: Honor, Not Location
Imam Razi explains two interpretations of 'thumma dana fatadalla' and refutes Pharaoh's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of divine nearness in Quran 53:8 and how Imam Razi refutes Pharaoh's spatial assumption about Allah.

Core Idea
Divine nearness is honor, not location.

Imam Razi tackles a key issue in divine transcendence: what does it mean for Allah to 'draw near'? The Quranic phrase 'thumma dana fatadalla' (Then he drew near and descended) might be misunderstood as implying spatial movement. Razi clarifies by offering two interpretations that safeguard Allah's transcendence, and then answers a doubt raised by Pharaoh's statement in the Quran. This lesson will help you navigate these crucial points.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first interpretation, second interpretation, then refutation of Pharaoh's doubt.
Pay attention to the evidence cited for each interpretation.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation is 'nearness of rank and honor' supported by Quran and hadith. The second is that the one who drew near is Jibreel, not Allah. Identify the evidence for each.
3
Understand the refutation of Pharaoh
Pharaoh asked for a tower to reach the Lord of Moses. Razi refutes by noting Moses said 'Lord of the heaven' not 'Lord in the heaven', and Pharaoh's assumption was mistaken.
This refutes the idea that Allah is spatially confined.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how both interpretations and the refutation uphold the principle of divine transcendence: Allah is not in a place.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن هذا الدنو دنو المنزله والكرامة . كقوله تعالى: « واسجد واقترب (۱۱۹) ، وقال عليه السلام - حكاية عن الله تعالى تقرب إلى شبراً تقربت إليه ذراعا ، د من [?] الثاني : . ثم دنا فتدلى ، أى جبريل دنا من محمد - عليهما السلام والدليل عليه : قوله تعالى في آية أخرى : ( ولقد رآه بالأفق المبين (۱۲۰)) ثم لما دنا جبريل من محمد عليهما السلام - حصل الوحى من الله تعالى إليه ولهذا قال (۱۳۱): ( فأوحى إلى عبده ما أوحى (۱۲۲) ، وأما الجواب عن التمسك بقول فرعون « يا هامان این لی صرحا (۱۲۲) فهو أن هذا الكلام من فرعون . وهو معارض بأن موسى - عليه السلام - لم : يقل الرب فى السماء ، بل قال : د رب السماء (۱۲۳) ، ثم إن فرعون كان قد ظن فيه أن الإله مستقر فى السماء . فهذا هو الجواب عن هذه الشبهة ) وبالله التوفيق (١٢٤))
The first: this nearness is the nearness of rank and honor. As He said, the Most High: 'Prostrate and draw near' (96:19), and the Prophet, peace be upon him, said — narrating from Allah, the Most High — 'Whoever draws near to Me by a span, I draw near to him by a cubit, [...]' The second: 'Then he drew near and descended' (53:8), meaning Jibreel drew near to Muhammad, peace be upon them. The evidence for it is His statement in another verse: 'And he indeed saw him in the clear horizon' (81:23). Then when Jibreel drew near to Muhammad, peace be upon them, revelation from Allah, the Most High, came to him, and therefore He said (53:10): 'So He revealed to His servant what He revealed.' As for the answer to the argument by the saying of Pharaoh: 'O Haman, build for me a tower' (40:36), this speech is from Pharaoh. And it is countered by the fact that Moses, peace be upon him, did not say 'the Lord is in the heaven' but rather said 'the Lord of the heaven' (26:?[?]). Then Pharaoh had assumed that the deity is settled in the heaven. So this is the answer to this doubt. And success is from Allah.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis presents two interpretations of the Quranic phrase 'thumma dana fatadalla' (Then he drew near and descended) from Surah an-Najm. The first interpretation, favored by the author, is that the nearness (deno) is not spatial but rather a nearness of station and honor (دنو المنزله والكرامة), supported by the verse from Surah al-Alaq 'Prostrate and draw near' and a prophetic tradition in which Allah says that whoever draws near to Him by a span, He draws near by a cubit. The second interpretation is that the one who drew near is Jibreel drawing near to the Prophet Muhammad, as evidenced by another verse where the Prophet saw Jibreel on the clear horizon. The passage then addresses a doubt (shubha) raised by the statement of Pharaoh: 'O Haman, build me a tower' (Surah Ghafir), which might imply that Pharaoh thought Allah is located in a spatial heaven. The refutation is that Moses did not say 'the Lord in the heaven' but rather 'the Lord of the heaven', indicating sovereignty rather than indwelling. Thus, Pharaoh's assumption was mistaken. The passage ends with 'And with Allah is success.'

Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that nearness (deno) in the Quranic context refers to honor and station, or to the angel Jibreel, not to Allah being in a place.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that Quranic references to divine nearness must be understood metaphorically as honor or as referring to the angel Jibreel, and that Pharaoh's spatial assumption is refuted by the distinction between 'Lord of the heaven' and 'Lord in the heaven'.

?

Ten Quranic Proofs that the Quran is Understandable
Razi lists ten Quranic verses to prove the Quran is fully understandable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cumulative scriptural argument from Imam Razi that the Quran must be fully intelligible, based on its own descriptions in ten verses.

Core Idea
The Quran's own attributes prove it is meant to be understood.

Imam Razi opens his discussion by asking whether the Quran can contain anything inaccessible to human knowledge. He reports a disagreement: some scholars allow it, but theologians deny it. To support the theologians, Razi presents ten Quranic proofs, each showing that a Quranic attribute—such as being 'clear', 'guidance', or a 'warning'—logically entails that the Quran is understandable. This lesson walks through each proof and reveals their common logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the full translation to get a sense of Razi's cumulative approach.
Notice that each proof is introduced by a number (first, second, etc.).
2
Identify the common logical pattern
Each proof takes a Quranic attribute (e.g., 'clear', 'guidance', 'proclamation') and argues that it necessitates the Quran being known and understood. Identify this pattern in at least three proofs.
3
Reflect on the first proof about tadabbur
The first proof uses the command to ponder (tadabbur). Ask: why would Allah command pondering if the Quran were not understandable? This is the simplest and strongest proof.
4
Consider the tenth proof's incompleteness
The tenth proof begins but is cut off. Reflect on how Razi might have completed it—likely the same pattern: the Quran is a 'balagh' (proclamation) so it must be understood.
The Arabic text ends abruptly at 'فكيف'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول

أنه هل يجوز أن يحصل في كتاب الله

اعلم : أن كثيرا من الفقهاء والمحدثين والصوفية ، يجوزون ذلك والمتكلمون ينكرونه . واحتجوا بالآيات والأخبار ) و المعقول (۱)) .

أحدها : قوله تعالى : « أفلا يتدبرون القرآن، أم على قلوب أقفاطا (۲)، أمر الناس بالتدير في القرآن . ولو كان القرآن غير مفهوم ، فكيف يأمرنا بالتدبر فيه ؟

The first chapter: On whether it is permissible that there occur in the Book of Allah, exalted is He, what there is no way for us to know? Know that many of the jurists, hadith scholars, and Sufis permit that, while the theologians (mutakallimun) deny it. And they argued with verses and reports and rational arguments. As for the verses, they are many. First: His saying, exalted: 'Do they not then ponder over the Quran? Or are there locks on their hearts?' [Muhammad 24] He commanded people to ponder over the Quran. And if the Quran were not understandable, how could He command us to ponder over it? Second: His saying, exalted: 'Do they not then ponder over the Quran? And if it had been from other than Allah, they would have found therein much discrepancy' [An-Nisa 82]. How could He command us to reflect on it to understand contradiction and discrepancy, while it is not understandable to creation? Third: His saying, exalted: 'And indeed it is a revelation of the Lord of the worlds, brought down by the Trustworthy Spirit upon your heart, that you may be among the warners, in a clear Arabic tongue' [Ash-Shu'ara 192-195]. And if it were not understandable, how could the messenger warn with it? And also His saying: 'in a clear Arabic tongue' [Ash-Shu'ara 195] indicates that it has been revealed in the Arabic language. And if that is so, it must be known. Fourth: His saying, exalted: 'those who derive it from them would know it' [An-Nisa 83]. Derivation from it is not possible except after comprehending its meaning. Fifth: His saying, exalted: 'a clarification for everything' [An-Nahl 89], and His saying: 'We have not neglected in the Book anything' [Al-An'am 38]. Sixth: His saying, exalted: 'guidance for the righteous' [Al-Baqarah 2]. And what is not known cannot be guidance. Seventh: His saying, exalted: 'far-reaching wisdom' [Al-Qamar 5], and His saying: 'and a healing for what is in the breasts, and a guidance and mercy for the believers' [Yunus 57]. And all these attributes are not attained in what is not known. Eighth: His saying, exalted: 'There has come to you from Allah a light and a clear Book' [Al-Ma'idah 15]. And it is not clear unless it is known. Ninth: His saying, exalted: 'Is it not sufficient for them that We have sent down to you the Book to be recited to them? Verily, in that is a mercy and a reminder' [Al-Ankabut 51]. How can the Book be sufficient, and how can it be a reminder, while it is not understandable? Tenth: His saying, exalted: 'This is a proclamation for mankind, and that they may be warned thereby' [Ibrahim 52]. How can [that be if it is not understandable?]
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an extended scriptural argument (istidlal bi al-ayat) from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis. The author sets up the first chapter's question: Is it possible that the Quran contains something inaccessible to human knowledge? He reports a disagreement: many jurists, hadith scholars, and Sufis allow this possibility, while the mutakallimun (theologians) deny it. The mutakallimun then present proofs: from verses, traditions, and reason. Razi begins with the scriptural arguments, listing ten Quranic verses. Each verse is cited and then briefly interpreted to show that the Quran presupposes its own intelligibility.

First verse (Muhammad 24): Allah commands pondering (tadabbur) over the Quran—an impossible command if the Quran were not understandable.
Second verse (An-Nisa 82): Again the command to ponder, this time to detect internal discrepancy. If the Quran were not understood, how could discrepancy be detected?
Third verse (Ash-Shu'ara 192-195): The Quran is described as a warning in a clear Arabic tongue. A warning must be understood to be effective. The phrase 'clear Arabic tongue' further implies it is comprehensible to its audience.
Fourth verse (An-Nisa 83): The Quran is a source from which rulings and knowledge are derived (istinbat). Derivation requires understanding the meaning.
Fifth verse (An-Nahl 89, Al-An'am 38): The Quran is a clarification of everything and nothing is neglected in it—implying comprehensive intelligibility.
Sixth verse (Al-Baqarah 2): The Quran is guidance (huda) for the righteous. Guidance can only come from something known.
Seventh verse (Al-Qamar 5, Yunus 57): The Quran is described as 'far-reaching wisdom', 'healing', 'guidance', 'mercy'. These qualities cannot apply to something unknown.
Eighth verse (Al-Ma'idah 15): The Quran is a 'clear Book' (kitab mubin). Clarity entails knowability.
Ninth verse (Al-Ankabut 51): The Quran is sufficient and a reminder—these functions require comprehension.
Tenth verse (Ibrahim 52): The Quran is a proclamation (balagh) and a warning—again, only effective if understood.

Each proof follows the same logical form: The Quran has a certain attribute or function that logically entails its knowability. If it were not understandable, that attribute would be pointless. Razi's argument is thus a cumulative case from the Quran's own self-description.

Key Terms
تدبر
تبيان
هدى
مبين
بلاغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is arguing that the Quran contains nothing obscure or difficult to understand.
Correction: Razi is only arguing against total unintelligibility of any part. He acknowledges that some verses may require interpretation but denies that any passage is entirely beyond human knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) The Quran is only for Arabs
B) The Quran must be known and comprehensible because it was revealed in a clear language
C) The Quran cannot be translated
D) The Quran is meant for recitation only

Key Takeaway
The Quran's own descriptions—such as being a 'clear book', 'guidance', and 'proclamation'—prove that it is entirely understandable and accessible to human reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Which of Razi's ten proofs strikes you most personally? Why does that particular attribute of the Quran fail to move you to action as it should? What would it take for that attribute to become real in your life?
?
The Quran calls itself a 'proclamation for mankind' (بلاغ للناس). If you knew with certainty that every verse is meant for you to understand and act upon, what would you do differently tomorrow morning when you open it?

Refutation of Two Parsing Possibilities for the Verse
Razi refutes two grammatical possibilities for a verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses grammatical reasoning to defend the Salaf position against speculative interpretation (ta'wīl).

Core Idea
Accepting the apparent meaning avoids the grammatical problems of speculative interpretation.

In this passage, Razi addresses a potential objection from those who defend interpretive liberty (ta'wīl) by proposing two grammatical readings of the verse about the firmly grounded in knowledge. Razi refutes both possibilities, showing that they either require unnecessary implied elements (iḍmār) or abandon the apparent meaning (ẓāhir). This lesson walks through his argument step by step, demonstrating how grammatical analysis supports the Salaf approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and two possibilities
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection presents two possible grammatical interpretations: one involving an implied predicate, and another treating the phrase as a circumstantial clause.
The first possibility: the phrase 'يقولون آمنا به' is an implied predicate for 'those who speak of interpretation'. The second: it is a circumstantial clause for the firmly grounded.
2
Understand the first refutation
Razi rejects the first possibility because it requires adding an implied element (iḍmār) to the verse. He argues that interpreting divine speech without such additions is always preferable.
The principle: interpreting without iḍmār is more appropriate than interpreting with it.
3
Understand the second refutation
Razi then shows that the second possibility is weak because it restricts the circumstantial clause to the firmly grounded, whereas the apparent meaning allows it to apply to both Allah and the firmly grounded. This is an abandonment of the literal sense.
Key point: a circumstantial clause must refer to the preceding subject(s). Since both Allah and the firmly grounded are mentioned, the apparent meaning leaves it open.
4
Grasp the overall conclusion
Razi concludes that the position permitting ta'wīl forces unnecessary iḍmār in this verse, while the Salaf position of accepting the straightforward meaning does not. Therefore, the Salaf position is grammatically and rationally superior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : في تصحيحه وجهار : الأول : أن يكون التقدير : هؤلاء القائلون بالتأويل ، ويقولون آمنا به . والثاني : أن يكون يقولون ، حالا من «الراسخين» قبل . أما الأول : فمدفوع . لأن تفسير كلام الله تعالى بما لا يحتاج معه إلى الإضمار ، أولى من تفسيره بما يحتاج معه إلى الإضمار . والثاني : ضعيف أيضا . لأن ذا الحال هو الذي تقدم ذكره ، وههنا تقدم ذكر الله (تعالى) وذكر الراسخين في العلم ، فوجب أن يكون قوله : (يقولون آمنا به) حالا من الراسخين ، لا من الله تعالى . فيكون ذلك تركا للظاهر . من حيث إن الظاهر يقتضى أن يكون ذلك حالا عن كل من تقدم ذكره فثبت أن القول بجواز التأويل محوج إلى الإضمار فى هذه الآية ، والقول بعدم جوازه لا يحوج إليه . فكان أولى .
If it is said: regarding its correction and permissibility: first: that the estimation is: these people who speak of interpretation, and they say 'we believe in it.' And second: that 'they say' is a circumstantial clause from 'the firmly grounded' before. As for the first: it is refuted, because interpreting the speech of Allah the Exalted with what does not require implication (iḍmār) is more appropriate than interpreting it with what requires implication. And the second: it is also weak, because the subject of the circumstantial clause is the one mentioned earlier, and here both Allah (Exalted) and the firmly grounded in knowledge are mentioned, so it must be that His saying 'they say we believe in it' is a circumstantial clause about 'the firmly grounded,' not about 'Allah the Exalted.' Thus that is an abandonment of the apparent meaning, since the apparent meaning requires that it be a circumstantial clause about everyone mentioned earlier. So it is established that the position permitting interpretation (ta'wīl) necessitates implication (iḍmār) in this verse, while the position prohibiting it does not necessitate it. So it is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection proposes two grammatical possibilities for parsing the verse about the firmly grounded in knowledge. The first possibility is that the phrase 'they say we believe in it' is an implied predicate for 'those who speak of interpretation.' The second possibility is that this phrase is a circumstantial clause (ḥāl) referring specifically to 'the firmly grounded.' Razi refutes both: the first is rejected because interpreting divine speech without an implied element is superior to implying unnecessary things. The second is weak because a circumstantial clause must refer to a previously mentioned subject, and here both Allah and the firmly grounded are mentioned; but connecting it to the firmly grounded rather than Allah disregards the literal sense, which would allow it to apply to both antecedents equally. From this, Razi concludes that allowing interpretive liberty (ta'wīl) forces one to add an implied element (iḍmār), whereas the Salaf position of accepting the apparent meaning without speculative interpretation avoids such hidden additions, making it the sounder approach.

Key Terms
تأويل
إضمار
حال
راسخون في العلم
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salaf position is merely traditional and lacks rational argument.
Correction: Razi provides a grammatical and logical proof that the Salaf position of accepting the apparent meaning is superior because it avoids unnecessary implied elements in divine speech.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the second possibility (making 'يقولون' a circumstantial clause for 'الراسخون') weak?
A) Because it requires an implied element in the verse.
B) Because it ignores the apparent meaning that the clause could apply to both Allah and the firmly grounded.
C) Because it is grammatically impossible.
D) Because it contradicts the consensus of the Salaf.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the Salaf position of accepting the apparent meaning is grammatically and logically superior because it avoids unnecessary implied elements in divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that seems ambiguous, do you immediately try to 'fix' it with your own interpretation, or do you trust that Allah's speech is clear even if you don't fully understand? This lesson shows that adding interpretations can force unnecessary implications. Is there an area of your life where you add complications because you don't accept the apparent command?
?
Have you ever preferred a complicated explanation because it seemed more sophisticated, while the simple truth was right in front of you? Razi argues that the simpler reading is grammatically superior. What does this teach you about humility before divine speech?
?
The Salaf position avoids speculation and adds no hidden meanings. Are there verses you have been tempted to reinterpret to fit your desires? Will you commit to accepting the apparent meaning unless there is clear proof otherwise?

The Fourth Argument: 'All is from Our Lord'
Razi enumerates the fourth argument from the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the verse 'All is from our Lord' as a fourth argument to support the Salaf position of not interpreting ambiguous verses in detail.

Core Idea
The verse itself shows the firmly grounded accept what they don't fully comprehend.

Razi presents a fourth argument derived directly from the Qur'an. He explains that the verse 'All is from our Lord' indicates that the firmly grounded in knowledge believe in both clear and ambiguous verses without demanding detailed interpretation. This supports the Salaf approach of refraining from speculative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's presentation of the argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage where Razi explains the meaning of the verse. Notice how he derives support for the Salaf position from the believers' response.
The verse is from Surah Al-Imran, verse 7.
2
Understand the logical implication
Razi argues that if the firmly grounded knew the detailed interpretation of the ambiguous verses, the verse would have no benefit. This suggests they do not know the details.
3
Connect to the Salaf position
Reflect on how this argument supports the Salaf school of avoiding detailed interpretation (ta'wil) of mutashabihat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : قوله تعالى : «كل من عند ربنا» يعنى : أنهم آمنوا بما عرفوه على التفصيل وبما لم يعرفوا تفصيله وتأويله . إذ لو كانوا عالمين بالتفصيل في الكلام ، لم يبق لهذا الكلام فائدة . فهذا أجمل وجوه الاستدلال بهذه الآية في نصرة مذهب السلف . فإن قيل : إن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم بإقامة الدليل على أن الوقف عند قوله تعالى : ( وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله ) واجب ، والعطف غير جاز . لأن هذا العطف قراءة مشهورة منقولة بالنقل المتواتر ، فإقامة الدليل على فساده طعن في النقل المتواتر ، وذلك لا يجوز . قيل : نحن لا نجعل هذه المسألة قطعية ، بل ظنية احتمالية . وعلى هذا التقدير يزول السؤال .
The fourth: His saying the Exalted: 'All is from our Lord' meaning: that they believed in what they knew in detail and what they did not know in detail and its interpretation. For if they were knowledgeable of the detail in the speech, no benefit would remain in this speech. So this is the most beautiful way of adducing this verse in support of the school of the Salaf. If it is said: This adduction is only completed by establishing the proof that stopping at His saying the Exalted 'And none knows its interpretation except God' is obligatory, and the conjunctive reading is not permissible. Because this conjunctive reading is a well-known transmitted reading via tawātur, so establishing a proof of its invalidity is an attack on the tawātur transmission, and that is not permissible. He said: We do not make this matter definitive, rather it is probable and conjectural. On this estimation, the objection is removed.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the verse and then answers a subsequent objection. The fourth proof is the verse 'All is from our Lord' (Quran 3:7). Razi explains that the firmly grounded in knowledge believe in both the clear verses they understand and the ambiguous verses they do not understand in detail. If they actually knew the detailed interpretation of the ambiguous verses, the verse would be pointless. Hence this supports the Salaf position of refraining from detailed interpretation. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning only works if one holds that the grammatical stop after 'God' (i.e., reading it as 'Only God knows its interpretation') is obligatory and the alternative reading with 'wa' (connecting 'those firmly grounded in knowledge' as also knowing) is invalid. But the conjunctive reading is mutawātir (massively transmitted), so arguing against it would undermine tawātur, which is impermissible. Razi answers that he does not treat this issue as definitive (qaṭʿī) but as probable (ẓannī). By conceding the possibility of the alternative reading, the argument is still valid as a probable proof, not a certainty. Thus the objection is resolved.

Key Terms
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, what does the verse 'All is from our Lord' indicate about the firmly grounded in knowledge?
A) They know the detailed interpretation of all verses.
B) They believe in what they understand and what they do not understand.
C) They reject the ambiguous verses.
D) They only believe in the clear verses.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth argument shows that the verse praises the firmly grounded for accepting both clear and ambiguous verses, implying they do not know the detailed interpretation of the latter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you genuinely content to accept matters of faith that you do not fully understand, or do you secretly feel uneasy when you cannot explain every detail?
?
When you encounter an ambiguous verse, does your heart immediately say 'All is from our Lord' with full submission, or does your mind demand an interpretation before you can feel at peace?
?
The firmly grounded are praised for their humility. Do you have that same humility before Allah's speech, or do you try to force explanations to satisfy your own intellect?

Answering the Objection: Probabilistic Proof
Razi answers an objection about the stop and conjunctive reading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against Razi's fourth argument based on the mutawātir conjunctive reading and his response that the argument is only probable, not definitive.

Core Idea
A probable proof suffices; it need not be definitive to support the Salaf position.

Having presented the fourth argument, Razi anticipates an objection: the argument requires rejecting the mutawātir conjunctive reading, which is impossible. Razi responds by clarifying that his argument is probabilistic, not definitive, so it does not require rejecting the transmitted reading.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection that Razi presents: the argument only works if the stop after 'God' is obligatory, but the conjunctive reading is mutawātir, so rejecting it would undermine tawātur.
The objection is a hypothetical: 'If it is said...'
2
Understand the challenge
Realize why this is a strong objection: the conjunctive reading is massively transmitted and considered definitive by many scholars. Arguing against it could be seen as attacking the integrity of Qur'anic transmission.
3
Read Razi's response
Razi responds by stating that he does not treat this matter as definitive (qat'i) but as probable (zanni). Thus, the argument is valid as a probable proof.
4
Appreciate the resolution
Notice how this avoids the objection: the argument does not claim certainty, so it does not contradict the mutawātir reading. It only provides a probable support for the Salaf position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : قوله تعالى : «كل من عند ربنا» يعنى : أنهم آمنوا بما عرفوه على التفصيل وبما لم يعرفوا تفصيله وتأويله . إذ لو كانوا عالمين بالتفصيل في الكلام ، لم يبق لهذا الكلام فائدة . فهذا أجمل وجوه الاستدلال بهذه الآية في نصرة مذهب السلف . فإن قيل : إن هذا الاستدلال إنما يتم بإقامة الدليل على أن الوقف عند قوله تعالى : ( وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله ) واجب ، والعطف غير جاز . لأن هذا العطف قراءة مشهورة منقولة بالنقل المتواتر ، فإقامة الدليل على فساده طعن في النقل المتواتر ، وذلك لا يجوز . قيل : نحن لا نجعل هذه المسألة قطعية ، بل ظنية احتمالية . وعلى هذا التقدير يزول السؤال .
The fourth: His saying the Exalted: 'All is from our Lord' meaning: that they believed in what they knew in detail and what they did not know in detail and its interpretation. For if they were knowledgeable of the detail in the speech, no benefit would remain in this speech. So this is the most beautiful way of adducing this verse in support of the school of the Salaf. Because this conjunctive reading is a well-known transmitted reading via tawātur, so establishing a proof of its invalidity is an attack on the tawātur transmission, and that is not permissible. He said: We do not make this matter definitive, rather it is probable and conjectural. On this estimation, the objection is removed.

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the verse and then answers a subsequent objection. The fourth proof is the verse 'All is from our Lord' (Quran 3:7). Razi explains that the firmly grounded in knowledge believe in both the clear verses they understand and the ambiguous verses they do not understand in detail. If they actually knew the detailed interpretation of the ambiguous verses, the verse would be pointless. Hence this supports the Salaf position of refraining from detailed interpretation. Then an objection is raised: this reasoning only works if one holds that the grammatical stop after 'God' (i.e., reading it as 'Only God knows its interpretation') is obligatory and the alternative reading with 'wa' (connecting 'those firmly grounded in knowledge' as also knowing) is invalid. But the conjunctive reading is mutawātir (massively transmitted), so arguing against it would undermine tawātur, which is impermissible. Razi answers that he does not treat this issue as definitive (qaṭʿī) but as probable (ẓannī). By conceding the possibility of the alternative reading, the argument is still valid as a probable proof, not a certainty. Thus the objection is resolved.

?

The Second Proof: Consensus of the Companions
Razi presents the second proof based on consensus of the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the consensus of the Companions to prove that detailed interpretation of ambiguous verses is impermissible.

Core Idea
The Companions' silence on interpretation is itself a proof.

Razi now moves from rational argument to communal evidence. He argues that the Companions—the most qualified interpreters—never engaged in detailed interpretation of ambiguous texts. Their unanimous restraint, given the abundance of such texts and strong motivation to understand them, becomes a powerful indicator that such interpretation is not allowed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the second proof. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: premise (abundant ambiguous texts + motivation) → conditional (if permissible, then Companions would have done it) → consequence (it would be well-known) → conclusion (since not reported, it is impermissible).
Focus on the chain of reasoning.
2
Identify the key premises
List the premises Razi uses: (1) Ambiguous verses are many. (2) Motives to investigate them were present. (3) If interpretation were permissible, the Companions—the most worthy—would have done it. (4) Their actions would have become widely known. (5) No such reports exist. Try to see how each premise supports the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the type of proof
This is a consensus (ijmāʿ) based argument. Ask yourself: Why is the Companions' collective silence considered authoritative? How does Razi establish that their silence was deliberate, not due to negligence?
The proof relies on the principle that if something had occurred, it would have been transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على صحة مذهب السلف : التمسك بإجماع الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - أن هذه المتشابهات في القرآن والأخبار : كثيرة . و الدواعي إلى البحث عنها ، والوقوف على حقائقها : متوفرة . فلو كان البحث عن تأويلها على سبيل التفصيل جائزا ، لكان أولى الخلق بذلك الصحابة والتابعون - رضى الله عنهم - ولو فعلوا ذلك لاشتهر ، ونقل بالتواتر . وحيث لم ينقل عن واحد من الصحابة والتابعين الخوض فيها ، علمنا أن الخوض فيها غير جائز . الحجة الثالثة : إنا قد ذكرنا أن اللفظ المتشابه قسمان: المجمل والمؤول . أما المجمل : فهو الذى يحتمل معنيين فصاعدا ، احتمالا على التسوية . فنقول : إنه إما أن يكون محتملا لمعنيين فقط ، أو لمعان أكثر من اثنين . فإن كان محتملا لمعنيين فقط ، ثم دل الدليل على عدم أحدهما ، فحينئذ تعين أن المراد هو الثاني . مثل : أن الفوق إما أن يراد به الفوق في الجهة أو في الرتبة . ولما بطل حمله على الجهة . تعينت الرتبة . أما إذا كان اللفظ لمفهومات ثلاثة لم يلزم من بطلان واحد منها تعين الثاني والثالث بعينه . ولا يمكن أيضا حمل اللفظ عليهما معا ، لما ثبت أن اللفظ المشترك ، لا يجوز استعماله في مفهوميه معا .
The second proof for the correctness of the school of the Salaf: adherence to the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions - may God be pleased with them - that these ambiguous verses and reports are many, and the motives to search for them and ascertain their realities are abundant. Thus if searching for their interpretation in detail were permissible, then the most worthy of creation for that would be the Companions and the Followers - may God be pleased with them. And if they had done that, it would have become well-known and transmitted via tawātur. Since it has not been transmitted from any of the Companions or Followers that they engaged in it, we know that engaging in it is not permissible. The third proof: We have mentioned that the ambiguous term (al-lafẓ al-mutashābih) is of two types: the summary (al-mujmal) and the interpreted (al-muʾawwal). As for the summary, it is that which admits two or more meanings with equal probability. Then we say: it is either admitting only two meanings, or more than two. If it admits only two meanings, and then evidence indicates the negation of one of them, then at that point it becomes specified that what is intended is the second. For example, 'above' (al-fawq) is either intended as above in direction or above in rank. Since carrying it upon direction is invalidated, rank becomes specified. As for if the term is for three concepts, the invalidation of one of them does not necessitate the specification of the second and the third specifically. And it is also not possible to carry the term upon both of them together, because it has been established that the shared term (al-lafẓ al-mushtarak) cannot be used for both of its meanings simultaneously.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct proofs for the Salaf position. The second proof is based on the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions. Razi argues that ambiguous verses (mutashābihāt) are numerous, and the natural human motivation to investigate and understand them was present. If detailed interpretation were allowed, the most qualified and eager people—the Companions and Followers—would have engaged in it. Had they done so, it would have become widely known and transmitted via tawātur. Since no such transmission exists from any of them, we conclude that engaging in such interpretation is impermissible. The third proof is a linguistic-logical argument classifying ambiguous words into two categories: mujmal (a word with multiple equally possible meanings) and muʾawwal (word that is interpreted based on evidence). Razi explains that when a mujmal word has only two possible meanings, and evidence eliminates one, the other is specified. The example is 'fawq' (above): it could mean spatial elevation or rank; when the spatial meaning is ruled out (since God is beyond direction), the rank meaning remains. However, if a word has three possible meanings, eliminating one does not specify which of the remaining two is intended, nor can the word be applied to both remaining meanings simultaneously because a shared term (mushtarak) cannot carry multiple meanings at once. This shows that without evidence to specify, such ambiguous terms cannot be resolved by speculative interpretation, supporting the Salaf's reluctance to assign definitive meanings without clear proof.

Key Terms
إجماع
صحابة
تابعون
متشابه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salaf position is mere blind following without evidence.
Correction: Razi shows that the Salaf position is based on a rational inference from the consensus of the Companions, which is itself a valid proof in Islamic legal theory.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, why is the absence of reports about Companions engaging in detailed interpretation significant?
A) Because the Companions were too busy with other matters.
B) Because if they had done it, it would have been transmitted widely due to abundant motives.
C) Because interpretation is always impermissible.
D) Because the Companions did not have access to the texts.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' unanimous silence on detailed interpretation is a proof from consensus that such interpretation is not allowed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an ambiguous verse in the Quran, do you feel a restless urge to find a detailed interpretation? What does this proof say about that desire, and how does it challenge your approach?
?
Are you willing to accept that there are aspects of Allah's revelation that you will never fully comprehend in this life? How does that acceptance humble you, and what practical change might it demand from your daily relationship with the Quran?
?
If the Companions, who were the most knowledgeable and pious, refrained from detailed interpretation, what does that imply about the safety of your own attempts? Will you commit to following their way, even when your mind craves explanations?

The Third Proof: Classification of Ambiguous Terms
Razi classifies ambiguous terms and analyzes logical consequences for interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's classification of ambiguous words into mujmal and mu'awwal, and the logical implications for specifying meanings.

Core Idea
Ambiguous words resist interpretation unless evidence eliminates all but one meaning.

Razi now presents a linguistic-logical proof. He first divides ambiguous terms (mutashābih) into two categories: mujmal (summary) and mu'awwal (interpreted). Then he focuses on mujmal, showing that when a word has only two possible meanings, evidence can specify one. But when there are three or more, eliminating one does not specify which of the remaining is intended, nor can the word be applied to all of them together. This supports the Salaf's caution in assigning definitive meanings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
Read the passage and note that Razi first mentions that mutashābih is of two types: mujmal and mu'awwal. Understand the definition: mujmal is a word that admits two or more meanings equally.
Razi does not define mu'awwal here, but it is implied as the opposite.
2
Follow the two-case analysis
Razi then examines two subcases: a word with exactly two possible meanings, and a word with three or more. For the two-meaning case, if evidence eliminates one, the other is specified. Example: 'above' could be spatial or rank; spatial is eliminated, so rank remains.
The example is key for understanding.
3
Understand the three-meaning case
If a word has three possible meanings and evidence eliminates one, the remaining two are not specified. Also, the word cannot be used for both remaining meanings simultaneously because a shared term cannot carry two meanings at once.
This shows ambiguity cannot be resolved without further evidence.
4
Connect to the Salaf position
Reflect on how this linguistic analysis supports the Salaf's refusal to assign definitive interpretations to ambiguous verses: without clear evidence, the meaning remains unsettled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على صحة مذهب السلف : التمسك بإجماع الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - أن هذه المتشابهات في القرآن والأخبار : كثيرة . و الدواعي إلى البحث عنها ، والوقوف على حقائقها : متوفرة . فلو كان البحث عن تأويلها على سبيل التفصيل جائزا ، لكان أولى الخلق بذلك الصحابة والتابعون - رضى الله عنهم - ولو فعلوا ذلك لاشتهر ، ونقل بالتواتر . وحيث لم ينقل عن واحد من الصحابة والتابعين الخوض فيها ، علمنا أن الخوض فيها غير جائز . الحجة الثالثة : إنا قد ذكرنا أن اللفظ المتشابه قسمان: المجمل والمؤول . أما المجمل : فهو الذى يحتمل معنيين فصاعدا ، احتمالا على التسوية . فنقول : إنه إما أن يكون محتملا لمعنيين فقط ، أو لمعان أكثر من اثنين . فإن كان محتملا لمعنيين فقط ، ثم دل الدليل على عدم أحدهما ، فحينئذ تعين أن المراد هو الثاني . مثل : أن الفوق إما أن يراد به الفوق في الجهة أو في الرتبة . ولما بطل حمله على الجهة . تعينت الرتبة . أما إذا كان اللفظ لمفهومات ثلاثة لم يلزم من بطلان واحد منها تعين الثاني والثالث بعينه . ولا يمكن أيضا حمل اللفظ عليهما معا ، لما ثبت أن اللفظ المشترك ، لا يجوز استعماله في مفهوميه معا .
The second proof for the correctness of the school of the Salaf: adherence to the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions - may God be pleased with them - that these ambiguous verses and reports are many, and the motives to search for them and ascertain their realities are abundant. Thus if searching for their interpretation in detail were permissible, then the most worthy of creation for that would be the Companions and the Followers - may God be pleased with them. And if they had done that, it would have become well-known and transmitted via tawātur. Since it has not been transmitted from any of the Companions or Followers that they engaged in it, we know that engaging in it is not permissible. The third proof: We have mentioned that the ambiguous term (al-lafẓ al-mutashābih) is of two types: the summary (al-mujmal) and the interpreted (al-muʾawwal). As for the summary, it is that which admits two or more meanings with equal probability. Then we say: it is either admitting only two meanings, or more than two. If it admits only two meanings, and then evidence indicates the negation of one of them, then at that point it becomes specified that what is intended is the second. For example, 'above' (al-fawq) is either intended as above in direction or above in rank. Since carrying it upon direction is invalidated, rank becomes specified. As for if the term is for three concepts, the invalidation of one of them does not necessitate the specification of the second and the third specifically. And it is also not possible to carry the term upon both of them together, because it has been established that the shared term (al-lafẓ al-mushtarak) cannot be used for both of its meanings simultaneously.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct proofs for the Salaf position. The second proof is based on the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions. Razi argues that ambiguous verses (mutashābihāt) are numerous, and the natural human motivation to investigate and understand them was present. If detailed interpretation were allowed, the most qualified and eager people—the Companions and Followers—would have engaged in it. Had they done so, it would have become widely known and transmitted via tawātur. Since no such transmission exists from any of them, we conclude that engaging in such interpretation is impermissible. The third proof is a linguistic-logical argument classifying ambiguous words into two categories: mujmal (a word with multiple equally possible meanings) and muʾawwal (word that is interpreted based on evidence). Razi explains that when a mujmal word has only two possible meanings, and evidence eliminates one, the other is specified. The example is 'fawq' (above): it could mean spatial elevation or rank; when the spatial meaning is ruled out (since God is beyond direction), the rank meaning remains. However, if a word has three possible meanings, eliminating one does not specify which of the remaining two is intended, nor can the word be applied to both remaining meanings simultaneously because a shared term (mushtarak) cannot carry multiple meanings at once. This shows that without evidence to specify, such ambiguous terms cannot be resolved by speculative interpretation, supporting the Salaf's reluctance to assign definitive meanings without clear proof.

Key Terms
متشابه
مجمل
مؤول
لفظ مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one meaning of an ambiguous word is impossible, the word automatically takes on a clear meaning.
Correction: Razi shows this is only true when the word has exactly two possible meanings. With three or more, eliminating one does not specify the others.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what happens when a mujmal word has three possible meanings and evidence eliminates one?
A) The word becomes clear and the remaining two meanings are both intended.
B) The remaining two meanings are not specifically indicated, and the word cannot be used for both at once.
C) The word must be interpreted as the first of the two remaining meanings.
D) The word becomes applied to the second meaning only.

Key Takeaway
The linguistic analysis shows that ambiguous terms cannot be assigned a definitive meaning without sufficient evidence, supporting the Salaf position of restraint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read verses about Allah's attributes (like 'Hand' or 'Above'), do you feel a personal tension between understanding them literally and accepting that Allah knows best? How does this lesson's linguistic principle challenge you to trust Allah's wisdom over your own reasoning?
?
In your own life, are you more comfortable with clear answers or with ambiguity? How does your attitude toward uncertainty in faith reflect your reliance on Allah? Will you commit to letting go of the need to always 'figure things out' and instead rest in tafwīḍ?
?
If a friend argues that they can interpret ambiguous verses using common sense, how would this lesson equip you to respond? Does your own practice align with the Salaf's restraint, or do you sometimes impose meanings without evidence? What will you change starting today?

First Branch: No Substitution of Ambiguous Terms
Imam Razi states the first branch: forbidding substitution of ambiguous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind the Salaf's prohibition against substituting ambiguous divine attributes with other ambiguous terms.

Core Idea
Even ambiguous terms differ in how much they suggest falsehood; changing them risks greater misguidance.

Imam al-Razi begins the first methodological branch: the prohibition of substituting one ambiguous term with another. He provides a rational argument based on the danger of increasing false suggestion and supports it with a legal analogy. This lesson explains the reasoning and its application to divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the precise language used for prohibition and precaution.
2
Identify the core ruling
Find the sentence stating the ruling: it is not permissible to replace one ambiguous word with another. This is the first branch.
The ruling applies whether the replacement is Arabic or Persian.
3
Understand the reason
Imam al-Razi gives the reason: some ambiguous terms are more suggestive of falsehood than others, and increasing false suggestion without need is prohibited.
Consider why even a slight increase in false suggestion matters when speaking about Allah.
4
Examine the analogy
He draws an analogy to the Islamic law of 'iddah, where precaution is applied broadly to avoid harming lineage rulings.
Note that caution in matters of Allah's attributes is even more important.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that if precaution is taken in worldly matters, it is more fitting to do so in theology.
This highlights the Salaf's cautionary approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنه لا يجوز تبديل لفظ من الألفاظ المتشابهة ، بلفظ آخر متشابه ، سواء كان بالعربية أو بالفارسية . وذلك لأن الألفاظ المتشابهة قد يكون بعضها أكثر إيهاما للباطل من البعض . والزيادة في الإيهام من غير حاجة إليها لا يجوز . بلى . قد تكون زيادة الإبهام حاصلة في اللفظين إلا أن التمييز بين هذا القسم، والقسم الأول فيه عسر . فالاحتياط : الامتناع من الكل . ألا ترى أن الشرع أوجب العدة على الموطوءة ، لبراءة الرحم، احتياطا لحكم النسب ، ثم قالوا : تجب العدة على العقيم، والآيسة ، وعند العزل . لأن بواطن الأرحام، لا يعلمها إلا علام الغيوب ؟ فإيجاب العدة أهون من ركوب الخطر . إلا أن الخطر في معرفة ذات الله تعالى وصفاته ، أعظم من الخطر في العدة . فإذا راعينا الاحتياط به، فلأن نراعيه ههنا أولى .
First: It is not permissible to replace one ambiguous word with another ambiguous word, whether in Arabic or Persian. That is because ambiguous words may be more suggestive of falsehood than others. And increasing suggestion (of falsehood) without need is not permissible. Rather, it may be that increased ambiguity occurs in both words, but distinguishing between this category and the first category is difficult. So caution dictates abstaining from all. Do you not see that the Law obligated the waiting period ('iddah) on the woman who has been sexually penetrated, to ensure the womb's emptiness, as a precaution for the ruling of lineage, and then they said: The 'iddah is obligatory on the barren woman, the post-menopausal woman, and in case of coitus interruptus, because the interiors of wombs none knows them except the Knower of the unseen? So imposing the 'iddah is easier than risking danger. Yet the danger in knowing the Essence of God Almighty and His attributes is greater than the danger in the 'iddah. If we accordingly observe precaution regarding it, then it is more fitting that we observe it here.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the first branch of the Salaf's methodology: the prohibition of substituting one ambiguous (mutashābih) term with another ambiguous term. Imam al-Razi begins by stating the ruling: it is not allowed to replace one ambiguous expression with another, regardless of language (Arabic or Persian). The reason is that some ambiguous terms are more suggestive (īhām) of false meaning than others. Increasing such suggestion without necessity is impermissible. He acknowledges that sometimes both terms have equal ambiguity, but distinguishing between cases of equal and unequal ambiguity is difficult, so the precautionary principle (iḥtiyāṭ) demands abstaining from all substitution. He then draws an analogy from Islamic law: the 'iddah (waiting period) is required for a woman after intercourse to ensure the womb is empty, as a precaution for lineage rulings. This precaution is applied even to women who cannot possibly be pregnant (barren, post-menopausal, or after coitus interruptus) because only God knows the hidden state of wombs. If such precaution is taken in worldly matters, then it is even more necessary in matters of God's essence and attributes, where the risk is far greater. Thus, the first branch forbids any substitution of ambiguous terms to avoid even potential misguidance.

Key Terms

الألفاظ المتشابهة
إيهام
احتياط
العدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substituting an ambiguous term with another is harmless if both are ambiguous.
Correction: Imam al-Razi argues that ambiguous terms differ in degree of false suggestion, so substitution may increase falsehood without need; therefore it is prohibited.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, why is it impermissible to substitute one ambiguous divine attribute expression with another?
A) Because the original term is always clearer.
B) Because ambiguous terms may differ in degree of suggesting falsehood, and increasing that without need is prohibited.
C) Because substitution is only allowed in Persian.
D) Because the Salaf never used ambiguous terms.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf prohibited substituting ambiguous terms because even slight increases in false suggestion are dangerous when speaking about Allah, and precaution (ihtiyat) demands avoiding all unnecessary substitution.

Imam al-Razi argues that precaution in matters of Allah's essence is even more important than precaution in lineage. What is one area of your faith where you have been lax in 'ihtiyat'?
?

Second Branch: Avoiding Derived Forms of Ambiguous Terms
Imam Razi explains why derived verbal forms of ambiguous terms are prohibited.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi prohibits deriving active participles from ambiguous divine attributes like 'istawā' and the linguistic principle behind it.

Core Idea
The active participle implies settledness, so we avoid it for God.

Imam al-Razi continues his method of safeguarding divine transcendence by laying down a second rule: we must avoid deriving active participle forms from ambiguous terms used for God. This lesson explains the linguistic reason from the science of rhetoric and a supporting Qur'anic parallel.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the ruling and the reasoning Imam Razi provides.
Pay attention to the parallel he draws from the teaching verses.
2
Understand the linguistic principle
Imam Razi states that in ʿilm al-bayān, the active participle (ism al-fāʿil) indicates firmness and stability of the attribute, whereas the verb form has a weaker indication. This is why we avoid saying 'mustawin' from 'istawā'.
This principle is key to preserving God's transcendence.
3
Examine the supporting parallel
Notice how the Qur'an uses verbs for God's teaching (ʿallama, ʿallamnā) but scholars unanimously forbid calling God 'muʿallim'. This shows consistency in avoiding active participles for divine attributes.
This parallel strengthens the argument.
4
Reflect on the underlying wisdom
Ask yourself: why is it necessary to avoid derived forms? How does this protect against anthropomorphism? Consider how the active participle might imply a settled attribute that does not befit Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع الثاني : إنه يجب الاحتراز عن التصريف فلا نقول في قوله تعالى : «استوى ، إنه مستوى لما ثبت في علم البيان : أن اسم الفاعل يدل على أن المشتق منه متمكنا ثابتا ومستقرا . أما لفظ الفعل فدلالته على هذا المعنى ضعيفة . والذي يؤكده : أنه ورد في القرآن أنه تعالى علم العباد . فقال : ( الرحمن علم القرآن ) و ( علمك ما لم تكن تعلم ) و ( علمناه من لدنا علما ) ، و ( وعلم آدم الأسماء كلها ) ، ثم أجمعنا على أنه لا يجوز أن يقال الله تعالى : يا معلم . فكذلك ههنا .
The second branch: It is obligatory to avoid derivation, so we do not say regarding His statement, the Most High: 'istawā' (He established Himself), that He is mustawin (established), because it is established in rhetoric (ʿilm al-bayān) that the active participle indicates that the derived form (i.e., the subject) is firmly settled and stable. As for the verb form, its indication of this meaning is weak. And what confirms it is that it came in the Qur'an that the Most High taught servants. He said: 'The Most Merciful taught the Qur'an' and 'He taught you what you did not know' and 'We taught him from Us a knowledge' and 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them.' Then we are unanimously agreed that it is not permissible to say 'O Teacher' (yā muʿallim) of God the Most High. So likewise here.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the second branch: avoiding the derivation (taṣrīf) of ambiguous terms. Specifically, from the verse 'istawā' (He established Himself), one should not say 'mustawin' (the one who establishes Himself in a settled manner). The reason is from the science of rhetoric (ʿilm al-bayān): the active participle (ism al-fāʿil) implies firmness, stability, and settlement (tamakkun, thubūt, istiqrār) of the attribute in its subject. The verb form, on the other hand, has a weaker indication of that settledness. Imam al-Razi supports this with a parallel: the Qur'an uses verbs of teaching (ʿallama, ʿallamnā, etc.) for God, but scholars unanimously agree that it is not permissible to address God as 'muʿallim' (Teacher) as an active participle, because that would imply a fixed attribute of teaching in a way that might not befit God's transcendence. Similarly, 'mustawin' from 'istawā' should be avoided. Thus, the second branch prohibits deriving active participles from ambiguous verbs to prevent implying corporeality or spatial location.

Key Terms
التصريف
اسم الفاعل
علم البيان
متمكنا ثابتا مستقرا
يا معلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since 'istawā' is used, 'mustawin' is also permissible.
Correction: The active participle carries stronger connotations of settledness and location, which are incompatible with divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Razi forbid saying 'mustawin' (established) for God?
A) Because the word 'mustawin' does not occur in the Qur'an.
B) Because the active participle implies firm settlement and stability, which suggests corporeality.
C) Because the Salaf never used derived forms.
D) Because the verb 'istawā' is ambiguous, but the active participle is clear.

Key Takeaway
Derived verbal forms like active participles should be avoided for ambiguous divine attributes because they imply a degree of settlement unsuitable for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
